The iddah period. Chapter (5) Subsidiary Sources: al-Istislah. That dissolution of marriage by the death of the husband obligated her to wait likewise four months and ten days. And if the woman is pregnant, her iddah extends until the delivery of the pregnancy in all circumstances. This is for Shari'i considerations, the most important of which is preserving lineages from loss through mixing; because if a woman marries after the dissolution of the previous marriage and then a pregnancy appears, it is not known from which of the two husbands it is? So this is a text that is definitive in its indication and transmission regarding obligation. Even if one imagines a maslahah in neglecting the iddah or reducing its duration, that does not stand as evidence against this definitive text. And it is not valid for a woman to marry before the completion of her iddah, because the intent of the Lawgiver is completely clear from the text, and He did not neglect considering the maslahah we imagine in the matter except because He sees in its place a greater corruption. And we have previously stated that every matter has two aspects of benefit and harm, and consideration is for the preponderant aspect (refer to: F 5/5).
22/5 - b
-
Maslahah towards the Non-Definitive Text:
As for when the text is non-definitive in its indication or transmission, then the ijtihads differ regarding the permissibility of restricting and specifying it with maslahah when there is conflict:
1 - The ijtihads that reject the theories of istihsan and istislah and refuse them, like the Shafi'i ijtihad, do not accept specifying the text - even if it is non-definitive - with maslahah, because whoever rejects the authority of al-maslahah al-mursalah in the absence of a text, when the text contradicts it, they reject it a fortiori.
But if a harm that arises hinders the path of applying the text, then the rule of necessities applies, and choosing the lesser of two evils, for necessities permit prohibitions.
Imam al-Ghazali holds in this regard that if circumstances arise that require, according to maslahah, contravening the Shari'i text, and adhering to the text would result in widespread general harm, then observing the maslahah here contrary to the requirement of the text is obligatory and there is no room for disagreement.
An example of that is: if the enemy combatants shield themselves with a group of our prisoners in their hands, and it is feared that leaving the enemy would enable them to overcome us, then it is permissible, rather obligatory, to shoot them with weapons, even if that results in killing those of our soldiers whose blood is inviolable by the definitive Qur'anic text. (Refer to: al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali, discussion of istislah).
And this example is agreed upon, and the Hanafi jurists have stated it, and they explained it by saying that if we do not strike out of concern for the lives of our prisoners who are shielded, the enemy will thereby gain control over us and then kill them and kill us. So sacrificing the prisoners to repel the enemy and save the people is the lesser of two evils. But in terms of intention, it is obligatory then for the archers, in terms of religious accountability, to intend by their shooting the enemy, those who are shielded from our group. (Refer to: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar (2233)).
Thus you see that the Shafi'is stipulate for acting upon maslahah contrary to the requirement of the text that the maslahah reaches the level of necessity, applying the rule of the lesser of two evils in exceptional emergent circumstances. And this, as we said, is a point of agreement even towards definitive texts. As for the normal general cases which are the subject of our discussion, there is no room for istislahi consideration in their view.
2 - As for the ijtihads that adopt the theory of istislah, there are two trends: The first trend does not accept specifying the text by virtue of al-maslahah al-mursalah even if the text is non-definitive, and this is the trend of Hanbali ijtihad. For the indication and generality of the text are given precedence in Ahmad ibn Hanbal's school over considering maslahah, because the place for considering it is only when there is no text, and there is no room for considering it when the text exists (refer to: Book of Ibn Hanbal by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Z)
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the relationship between maslahah (public interest) and textual evidence (nusus) in Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on when maslahah can override a text. The author first presents the definitive text (qat'i) of the iddah period, establishing that no maslahah can override a qat'i text. Then he moves to non-definitive texts, explaining the Shafi'i and Hanbali positions. The Hanafi view is illustrated through the example of tatarruq (enemy using Muslim prisoners as human shields), where necessity (darurah) permits overriding the prohibition of killing innocent Muslims. The commentary should explain each part in order.

The author begins by citing the ruling of 'iddah (waiting period) for a widow: four months and ten days, or until delivery if pregnant. This ruling is based on definitive texts (Qur'an 2:234 and 2:228). The author stresses that even if one imagines a maslahah in shortening or abandoning the 'iddah, such as to facilitate remarriage, the text cannot be set aside because the Lawgiver has already weighed the considerations and determined that ignoring the 'iddah leads to greater harm (loss of lineage). Thus, a qat'i text prevails over any speculative maslahah.

Then the discussion shifts to non-definitive texts (ghayr qat'i), where there is room for ijtihad. Three schools are mentioned:
1. Shafi'i: They reject istihsan and istislah entirely, so they do not allow maslahah to restrict even a non-definitive text. However, if applying the text leads to a harmful consequence, the rules of darurah (necessity) and choosing the lesser of two evils apply. Imam al-Ghazali (a Shafi'i himself) acknowledged that if adhering to a text causes widespread general harm, then maslahah overrides it necessarily. The example of tatarruq is given: when the enemy shields themselves with Muslim prisoners, it is permissible—even obligatory—to attack, despite the inviolability of the prisoners' lives, to prevent a greater harm (the enemy's victory and killing of many).
2. Hanbali: They also prioritize the text over maslahah, even if the text is non-definitive, and only consider maslahah in the absence of a text.
3. Hanafi: The text shows that the Hanafis agree with the tatarruq example, citing al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar. They reason that sacrificing a few prisoners to save the entire ummah is the lesser of two harms. But they also add a condition of intention: the archers must intend to target the enemy, not the prisoners, in their hearts.

The author concludes that Shafi'is allow maslahah to override a text only in cases of necessity, even with qat'i texts; Hanbalis do not allow it for non-definitive texts; the Hanafi position is implicit in the agreement with the tatarruq example, showing that necessity can override even a qat'i text, but not for arbitrary maslahah.

Key Terms
النص القطعي (al-nass al-qat'i): A text that is definitive both in its transmission (thubut) and its meaning (dalalah), leaving no room for interpretation.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the dissolution of marriage (by death or divorce) before she can remarry.
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslahah al-mursalah): Considerations of public interest that are not explicitly recognized or rejected by the primary sources, used as a basis for legal rulings in the absence of specific textual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why cannot a speculative maslahah override a qat'i text like the iddah ruling?
A) Because the text is from the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Because the Lawgiver has already weighed the harms and benefits and the text reflects the preponderant consideration.
C) Because maslahah mursalah is not recognized by any school.
D) Because the iddah is a recommended act, not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
A definitive text (qat'i) cannot be overridden by a speculative maslahah; only darurah (necessity) can permit an exception, and that is based on a different principle.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Maslahah and Non-Definitive Texts: Schools' Positions and the Tatarruq Example
Non-definitive text and maslahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Shafi'i, Hanbali, and Hanafi positions on using maslahah to restrict a non-definitive text, and apply the principle of darurah in the tatarruq scenario.

Core Idea
For non-definitive texts, schools differ on maslahah's role; but all allow necessity (darurah) to override even definitive texts in extreme cases.

This lesson delves into the more nuanced realm where the text is not definitive (ghayr qat'i). Here, mujtahids differ on whether maslahah can specify or restrict the text. We examine the Shafi'i and Hanbali rejections of this practice, and the Hanafi position illustrated through the classic case of tatarruq (enemy using Muslim prisoners as shields). Understanding these differences is crucial for a mufti to know when public interest can be given weight and when it must yield to textual authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify a non-definitive text
A text that is either not definitively transmitted (zanni al-thubut) or not definitively clear in meaning (zanni al-dalalah). Examples include solitary hadiths (ahad) or verses with ambiguous wording.
Such texts allow room for ijtihad.
2
Summarize the Shafi'i position
Shafi'is reject istihsan and istislah entirely. Thus, they do not allow maslahah to specify or restrict a non-definitive text. However, if applying the text leads to widespread harm, the principle of darurah (necessity) applies, and the text may be set aside.
Imam al-Ghazali (a Shafi'i) confirmed this exception.
3
Summarize the Hanbali position
Hanbalis accept al-maslahah al-mursalah only in the absence of any text. If a text exists, even if non-definitive, the text prevails over maslahah.
This is stricter than the Shafi'i position regarding non-definitive texts.
4
Analyze the tatarruq example
The enemy shields themselves with Muslim prisoners. The definitive text prohibits killing innocent Muslims. However, if the enemy is not attacked, they will kill more people and conquer. Here, necessity (darurah) dictates that attacking is obligatory, choosing the lesser harm (sacrificing a few to save many).
This example is agreed upon by all schools, including Hanafis.
5
Note the Hanafi condition on intention
Hanafi jurists add that the archers must intend to target the enemy, not the prisoners, in their hearts. This preserves the principle that while the act is permissible, the intention must be correct.
This is from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar.

۲ - أما الاجتهادات التي تأخذ بنظرية الاستصلاح، ففيها اتجاهان : الاتجاه الأول : لا يقبل تخصيص النص بمقتضى المصلحة المرسلة

فدلالة النص وعمومه مقدَّمان في مذهب أحمد بن حنبل على النظر إلى المصلحة، لأنَّ محل النظر إليها إنما هو عند فقدان النص، ولا مجال للنظر إليها عند وجوده (ر ) كتاب ابن حنبل للأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبي ر)
The iddah period. Chapter (5) Subsidiary Sources: al-Istislah. That dissolution of marriage by the death of the husband obligated her to wait likewise four months and ten days. And if the woman is pregnant, her iddah extends until the delivery of the pregnancy in all circumstances. This is for Shari'i considerations, the most important of which is preserving lineages from loss through mixing; because if a woman marries after the dissolution of the previous marriage and then a pregnancy appears, it is not known from which of the two husbands it is? So this is a text that is definitive in its indication and transmission regarding obligation. Even if one imagines a maslahah in neglecting the iddah or reducing its duration, that does not stand as evidence against this definitive text. And it is not valid for a woman to marry before the completion of her iddah, because the intent of the Lawgiver is completely clear from the text, and He did not neglect considering the maslahah we imagine in the matter except because He sees in its place a greater corruption. And we have previously stated that every matter has two aspects of benefit and harm, and consideration is for the preponderant aspect (refer to: F 5/5).
22/5 - b
-
Maslahah towards the Non-Definitive Text:
As for when the text is non-definitive in its indication or transmission, then the ijtihads differ regarding the permissibility of restricting and specifying it with maslahah when there is conflict:
1 - The ijtihads that reject the theories of istihsan and istislah and refuse them, like the Shafi'i ijtihad, do not accept specifying the text - even if it is non-definitive - with maslahah, because whoever rejects the authority of al-maslahah al-mursalah in the absence of a text, when the text contradicts it, they reject it a fortiori.
But if a harm that arises hinders the path of applying the text, then the rule of necessities applies, and choosing the lesser of two evils, for necessities permit prohibitions.
Imam al-Ghazali holds in this regard that if circumstances arise that require, according to maslahah, contravening the Shari'i text, and adhering to the text would result in widespread general harm, then observing the maslahah here contrary to the requirement of the text is obligatory and there is no room for disagreement.
An example of that is: if the enemy combatants shield themselves with a group of our prisoners in their hands, and it is feared that leaving the enemy would enable them to overcome us, then it is permissible, rather obligatory, to shoot them with weapons, even if that results in killing those of our soldiers whose blood is inviolable by the definitive Qur'anic text. (Refer to: al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali, discussion of istislah).
And this example is agreed upon, and the Hanafi jurists have stated it, and they explained it by saying that if we do not strike out of concern for the lives of our prisoners who are shielded, the enemy will thereby gain control over us and then kill them and kill us. So sacrificing the prisoners to repel the enemy and save the people is the lesser of two evils. But in terms of intention, it is obligatory then for the archers, in terms of religious accountability, to intend by their shooting the enemy, those who are shielded from our group. (Refer to: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar (2233)).
Thus you see that the Shafi'is stipulate for acting upon maslahah contrary to the requirement of the text that the maslahah reaches the level of necessity, applying the rule of the lesser of two evils in exceptional emergent circumstances. And this, as we said, is a point of agreement even towards definitive texts. As for the normal general cases which are the subject of our discussion, there is no room for istislahi consideration in their view.
2 - As for the ijtihads that adopt the theory of istislah, there are two trends: The first trend does not accept specifying the text by virtue of al-maslahah al-mursalah even if the text is non-definitive, and this is the trend of Hanbali ijtihad. For the indication and generality of the text are given precedence in Ahmad ibn Hanbal's school over considering maslahah, because the place for considering it is only when there is no text, and there is no room for considering it when the text exists (refer to: Book of Ibn Hanbal by Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Z)
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the relationship between maslahah (public interest) and textual evidence (nusus) in Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on when maslahah can override a text. The author first presents the definitive text (qat'i) of the iddah period, establishing that no maslahah can override a qat'i text. Then he moves to non-definitive texts, explaining the Shafi'i and Hanbali positions. The Hanafi view is illustrated through the example of tatarruq (enemy using Muslim prisoners as human shields), where necessity (darurah) permits overriding the prohibition of killing innocent Muslims. The commentary should explain each part in order.

The author begins by citing the ruling of 'iddah (waiting period) for a widow: four months and ten days, or until delivery if pregnant. This ruling is based on definitive texts (Qur'an 2:234 and 2:228). The author stresses that even if one imagines a maslahah in shortening or abandoning the 'iddah, such as to facilitate remarriage, the text cannot be set aside because the Lawgiver has already weighed the considerations and determined that ignoring the 'iddah leads to greater harm (loss of lineage). Thus, a qat'i text prevails over any speculative maslahah.

Then the discussion shifts to non-definitive texts (ghayr qat'i), where there is room for ijtihad. Three schools are mentioned:
1. Shafi'i: They reject istihsan and istislah entirely, so they do not allow maslahah to restrict even a non-definitive text. However, if applying the text leads to a harmful consequence, the rules of darurah (necessity) and choosing the lesser of two evils apply. Imam al-Ghazali (a Shafi'i himself) acknowledged that if adhering to a text causes widespread general harm, then maslahah overrides it necessarily. The example of tatarruq is given: when the enemy shields themselves with Muslim prisoners, it is permissible—even obligatory—to attack, despite the inviolability of the prisoners' lives, to prevent a greater harm (the enemy's victory and killing of many).
2. Hanbali: They also prioritize the text over maslahah, even if the text is non-definitive, and only consider maslahah in the absence of a text.
3. Hanafi: The text shows that the Hanafis agree with the tatarruq example, citing al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar. They reason that sacrificing a few prisoners to save the entire ummah is the lesser of two harms. But they also add a condition of intention: the archers must intend to target the enemy, not the prisoners, in their hearts.

The author concludes that Shafi'is allow maslahah to override a text only in cases of necessity, even with qat'i texts; Hanbalis do not allow it for non-definitive texts; the Hanafi position is implicit in the agreement with the tatarruq example, showing that necessity can override even a qat'i text, but not for arbitrary maslahah.

Key Terms
النص غير القطعي (al-nass ghayr al-qat'i): A text that is either not definitively transmitted (zanni al-thubut) or not definitively clear in meaning (zanni al-dalalah), allowing for ijtihad.
الضرورة (al-darurah): A situation of extreme necessity that permits violating a normal legal prohibition to prevent a greater harm.
التترس (al-tatarruq): The act of an enemy using innocent people (especially Muslim prisoners) as human shields to protect themselves from attack.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Shafi'i position on using maslahah with a non-definitive text in normal circumstances?
A) They allow it if the maslahah is clear and overriding.
B) They reject it because they reject istihsan and istislah entirely.
C) They consider it on a case-by-case basis.
D) They prefer maslahah over the text in non-definitive cases.

Key Takeaway
For non-definitive texts, schools disagree on using maslahah, but all allow the principle of darurah to override even definitive texts in extreme emergencies, such as the tatarruq example.

Hanafi Use of Maslahah to Specify Texts
Arguing that Hanafi school uses maslahah to specify texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi school's use of maslahah mursalah to specify general texts, using the example of hearsay testimony to apply the concept.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh uses maslahah under istihsan to specify general texts, as in accepting hearsay to protect endowments.

This passage challenges the common assumption that only the Maliki school employs maslahah mursalah. The author demonstrates that Hanafi rulings, particularly the acceptance of hearsay testimony for ancient endowments, show the school's practical use of public interest to specify general texts, often under the rubric of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
Recognize the claim that only the Maliki school uses maslahah to specify texts, as stated by some researchers and Sheikh Abu Zahra.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Examine the hadith on testimony
Read the hadith: 'On the like of it testify or refrain,' which apparently requires direct sight (عيان) for testimony.
This hadith is the general text that Hanafi specify.
3
See how Hanafi specify via maslahah
Hanafi jurists accept hearsay testimony (شهادة التسامع) for old endowments, contradicting the literal meaning of the hadith, because requiring direct witnesses would lead to loss of rights.
They justify this by istihsan based on maslahah and necessity.
4
List other accepted cases
The text mentions lineage, death, and consummation of marriage as other matters where hearsay is accepted.
This shows the principle is applied broadly.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
The author concludes that the Hanafi school effectively uses maslahah mursalah, even if termed istihsan, and preceded the Maliki school in practical application.
This refutes the common misconception.

الباب الثاني : مصادر الفقه الإسلامي

والمشهور بين الباحثين أن المذهب المالكي هو الذي يرى تخصيص النصوص بالمصلحة عند التعارض دون المذهب الحنفي. وهذا أيضاً رأي الأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبي زهرة في كتابه «مالك و ابن حنبل».
ولكن هذا في نظرنا خلاف الواقع، فإنَّ فروع مذهب الحنفية والأحكام التي قرروها فيه تشهد بأنهم سبقوا الاجتهاد المالكي في تخصيص النصوص بالمصلحة المرسلة، كما يتضح مما يلي :
٢٤/٥ - في المذهب الحنفي :
١ - ورد في السنة أنَّ النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام سئل عن الشهادة فقال للسائل : هل ترى الشمس؟ قال : نعم قال: «على مثلها فاشهد أو دع ».
وهذا بظاهره يوجب العيان في تحمل الشهادة في جميع الشؤون عامة، ويحظر على الإنسان أن يشهد أمام القضاء بأمر لم يشاهده وإنما سمع به من غيره.
وقد قرر الفقهاء بناءً على هذا عدم قبول شهادة التسامع في إثبات الحقوق. لكن فقهاء الحنفية رأوا أنَّ هناك موضوعات تقتضي المصلحة فيها قبول شهادة التسامع، لأنَّ اشتراط العيان فيها متعذر أو غير متيسر، فيضيع هذا الاشتراط حقوقاً هامة لا يسوغ الشرع التفريط فيها، فقرروا قبول شهادة التسامع في أمور عديدة منها إثبات أصل الوقف أي إثبات أن هذا العقار موقوف وليس ملكاً لصاحب اليد عند الاختلاف في وقفيته وملكيته .
وقد عللوا ذلك بأن المصلحة تقضي بقبول شهادة التسامع هنا استحساناً على خلاف القياس للضرورة، وذلك صيانة للأوقاف القديمة عن الضياع، لأن الوقف إذا تقادم ولم تكن عقاراته مسجلة في سجل المحكمة، أو فقد سجلها، لا يبقى ممكناً إثبات وقفيتها لانقراض الشهود الذين شهدوا على عبارة الواقف حينما وقف الوقف فيتجرأ كل إنسان على غضب الأوقاف القديمة وادعاء ملكيتها دون إمكان إثبات وقفيتها إذا اشترطنا العيان. فلذا تقبل شهادة التسامع في هذا الإثبات وذلك بأن يشهد الشاهد بالوقفية استناداً إلى ما يسمعه من الناس الثقات ولو أنهم أيضاً سامعون من مثلهم وغير معاينين ومما قبل فيه أيضاً فقهاء الحنفية شهادة التسامع: إثبات النسب، والوفاة، والدخول بالزوجة إلى عشرة مواضع مبينة في كتبهم، فخصصوا بذلك نص الحديث النبوي الذي يوجب العيان في تحمل الشهادة ليصح
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence

It is well-known among researchers that the Maliki school is the one that holds the opinion of specifying texts by maslahah when there is conflict, unlike the Hanafi school. This is also the view of Professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra in his book 'Malik and Ibn Hanbal.' But this, in our view, is contrary to reality, for the branches of the Hanafi school and the rulings they established in it testify that they preceded Maliki ijtihad in specifying texts by maslahah mursalah, as becomes clear from the following:
24/5 - In the Hanafi school:
1 - It is reported in the Sunnah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about testimony, and he said to the questioner: 'Do you see the sun?' He said: Yes. He said: 'On the like of it testify, or refrain.' This, in its apparent meaning, requires direct sight in undertaking testimony in all general matters, and prohibits a person from testifying before the court about something he did not witness but only heard from others. Based on this, the jurists ruled the non-acceptance of hearsay testimony in establishing rights. However, the Hanafi jurists saw that there are matters where maslahah requires the acceptance of hearsay testimony, because the condition of direct sight is impossible or not feasible, and this condition would cause the loss of important rights that Shari'ah does not permit neglecting. So they ruled for the acceptance of hearsay testimony in many matters, including establishing the origin of an endowment (waqf), i.e., establishing that this property is a waqf and not the property of the possessor when there is dispute over its waqf status and ownership. They justified this by saying that maslahah necessitates accepting hearsay testimony here as istihsan (juristic preference) contrary to qiyas, due to necessity, in order to protect ancient endowments from loss. Because if the waqf is old and its properties are not registered in the court registry, or its registry is lost, it becomes impossible to prove its waqf status due to the extinction of the witnesses who testified to the founder's expression when the waqf was established. Then every person would dare to seize ancient endowments and claim ownership without the possibility of proving their waqf status if we require direct sight. Therefore, hearsay testimony is accepted in this proof, by the witness testifying to the waqf status based on what he hears from reliable people, even if they are also hearers from their like and not direct witnesses. Among what the Hanafi jurists also accepted hearsay testimony for: establishing lineage, death, and consummation of marriage (dukhul) up to ten places detailed in their books. Thus they specified the text of the prophetic hadith that requires direct sight in undertaking testimony to make it valid.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy argument demonstrating that the Hanafi school, contrary to popular belief, does use maslahah mursalah (public interest) to specify general texts. The author refutes the claim that only the Maliki school does so. He provides a concrete example: the general hadith requiring direct sight for testimony is specified by Hanafi jurists in cases where necessity demands accepting hearsay testimony (shahadat al-tasamu'). The example given is establishing the status of an old endowment (waqf). Because requiring direct witnesses would lead to the loss of awqaf, the Hanafi school accepts hearsay based on maslahah, using istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas. The passage then lists other instances where hearsay is accepted: lineage, death, and consummation of marriage. This shows that the Hanafi school practically applies maslahah to specify texts, just like the Maliki school, but often under the name of istihsan.

Key Terms
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslahah al-mursalah): Consideration of public interest that is not explicitly regulated by a specific textual evidence, used to specify general texts when there is a conflict.
شهادة التسامع (shahadat al-tasamu'): Testimony based on hearsay, where the witness reports what he has heard from others rather than direct observation.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that leads to departing from a clear analogy (qiyas) for a more suitable ruling based on a stronger evidence or necessity, often rooted in maslahah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, hearsay testimony (shahadat al-tasamu') is accepted in which of the following matters due to public interest?
A) Establishing the ownership of a recently purchased house
B) Proving the existence of an ancient endowment (waqf)
C) Testifying to a contract witnessed directly
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses maslahah mursalah under the name of istihsan to specify general texts in order to prevent the loss of rights and properties.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Maslahah Mursalah as a Takhsis Tool in Hanafi Fiqh
Explaining how maslahah mursalah justifies exceptional rulings in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Hanafi jurists use maslahah mursalah to specify general proofs (takhsis) and apply this mechanism to evaluate fatwa scenarios involving public interest.

Core Idea
Maslahah mursalah allows Hanafis to specify general texts when strict application harms public welfare.

The passage explores how Hanafi scholars, while generally not treating maslahah mursalah as an independent source, do use it to create exceptions to general rules. Through three vivid examples—hearsay testimony, fruit sales, and women's testimony—we see how necessity and public interest can override literal textual demands. This lesson unpacks the mechanism and its application in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its textual basis
In each example, first determine what the default ruling is from the primary texts (Quran, Sunnah). For hearsay, the rule is that testimony requires direct observation. For sales, selling non-existent items or gharar is prohibited. For testimony, the default requires male witnesses.
The texts are explicit general prohibitions.
2
Assess the hardship or loss from strict application
Consider what would happen if the rule were applied strictly. For old endowments, requiring direct eyewitnesses would make it impossible to prove waqf status, leading to loss of charitable property. For fruit sales, people need to sell crops before full harvest. For women's matters, requiring male witnesses would leave many incidents unproven.
The need (ḥājah) or necessity (ḍarūrah) is key.
3
See how maslahah mursalah provides justification for the exception
There is no specific text allowing these exceptions, but the general principle of preserving public interest (maslahah) justifies them. The Hanafis call this istiḥsān bi al-ḍarūrah or takhṣīṣ based on maslahah.
Maslahah here is unrestricted (mursalah) – not supported by a specific text but also not contradicted.
4
Understand the Hanafi framework: takhsis via necessity
The Hanafis do not accept maslahah as an independent source like the Malikis, but they use it to specify the scope of general proofs (takhṣīṣ al-ʿumūm). This preserves the authority of the texts while allowing flexibility.
The exceptions are limited and well-defined.
5
Apply the model to new cases
When faced with a modern scenario that involves public interest but no specific text, analyze whether strict application would lead to hardship and whether the interest is genuine and not contradicting a clear text. If so, consider a takhsis based on maslahah.
Always stay within the Hanafi hierarchy of sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْفَصْل (٥) الْمَصَادِرُ التَّبَعِيَّةُ الِاسْتِصْلَاحُ

عامَّةً، وَيُحْظَرُ عَلَى الْإِنْسَانِ أَنْ يَشْهَدَ أَمَامَ الْقَضَاءِ بِأَمْرٍ لَمْ يُشَاهِدْهُ وَإِنَّمَا سَمِعَ بِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.

وَقَدْ قَرَّرَ الْفُقَهَاءُ بِنَاءً عَلَى هَذَا عَدَمَ قَبُولِ شَهَادَةِ التَّسَامُعِ فِي إِثْبَاتِ الْحُقُوقِ. لَكِنَّ فُقَهَاءَ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ رَأَوْا أَنَّ هُنَاكَ مَوْضُوعَاتٍ تَقْتَضِي الْمَصْلَحَةُ فِيهَا قَبُولَ شَهَادَةِ التَّسَامُعِ، لِأَنَّ اشْتِرَاطَ الْعِيَانِ فِيهَا مُتَعَذِّرٌ أَوْ غَيْرُ مُيَسَّرٍ، فَيَضِيعُ هَذَا الِاشْتِرَاطُ حُقُوقًا هَامَّةً لَا يُسَوِّغُ الشَّرْعُ التَّفْرِيطَ فِيهَا، فَقَرَّرُوا قَبُولَ شَهَادَةِ التَّسَامُعِ فِي أُمُورٍ عَدِيدَةٍ مِنْهَا إِثْبَاتُ أَصْلِ الْوَقْفِ: أَيْ إِثْبَاتُ أَنَّ هَذَا الْعَقَارَ مَوْقُوفٌ وَلَيْسَ مِلْكًا لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ عِنْدَ الِاخْتِلَافِ فِي وَقْفِيَّتِهِ وَمِلْكِيَّتِهِ. وَقَدْ عَلَّلُوا ذَلِكَ بِأَنَّ الْمَصْلَحَةَ تَقْضِي بِقَبُولِ شَهَادَةِ التَّسَامُعِ هُنَا اسْتِحْسَانًا عَلَى خِلَافِ الْقِيَاسِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ، وَذَلِكَ صِيَانَةً لِلْأَوْقَافِ الْقَدِيمَةِ عَنِ الضَّيَاعِ، لِأَنَّ الْوَقْفَ إِذَا تَقَادَمَ وَلَمْ تَكُنْ عَقَارَاتُهُ مُسَجَّلَةً فِي سِجِلِّ الْمَحْكَمَةِ، أَوْ فُقِدَ سِجِلُّهَا، لَا يَبْقَى مُمْكِنًا إِثْبَاتُ وَقْفِيَّتِهَا لِانْقِرَاضِ الشُّهُودِ الَّذِينَ شَهِدُوا عَلَى عِبَارَةِ الْوَاقِفِ حِينَمَا وَقَفَ الْوَقْفَ، فَيَتَجَرَّأُ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ عَلَى غُضْبِ الْأَوْقَافِ الْقَدِيمَةِ وَادِّعَاءِ مِلْكِيَّتِهَا دُونَ إِمْكَانِ إِثْبَاتِ وَقْفِيَّتِهَا إِذَا اشْتَرَطْنَا الْعِيَانَ. فَلَذَا تُقْبَلُ شَهَادَةُ التَّسَامُعِ فِي هَذَا الْإِثْبَاتِ، وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ يَشْهَدَ الشَّاهِدُ بِالْوَقْفِيَّةِ اسْتِنَادًا إِلَى مَا يَسْمَعُهُ مِنَ النَّاسِ الثِّقَاتِ وَلَوْ أَنَّهُمْ أَيْضًا سَامِعُونَ مِنْ مِثْلِهِمْ وَغَيْرُ مُعَايِنِينَ. وَمِمَّا قُبِلَ فِيهِ أَيْضًا فُقَهَاءُ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ شَهَادَةُ التَّسَامُعِ: إِثْبَاتُ النَّسَبِ، وَالْوَفَاةِ، وَالدُّخُولِ بِالزَّوْجَةِ، إِلَى عَشَرَةِ مَوَاضِعَ مُبَيَّنَةٍ فِي كُتُبِهِمْ (٢)، فَخَصَّصُوا بِذَلِكَ نَصَّ الْحَدِيثِ النَّبَوِيِّ الَّذِي يُوجِبُ الْعِيَانَ فِي تَحَمُّلِ الشَّهَادَةِ لِيَصِحَّ

(1) يُلْحَظُ فِي هَذَا الْمَقَامِ أَنَّهُ بَعْدَ إِنْشَاءِ السِّجِلِّ الْعَقَارِيِّ فِي بِلَادِنَا أَصْبَحَتْ قُيُودُهُ هِيَ الْمُعَوَّلُ عَلَيْهَا فِي إِثْبَاتِ الْمِلْكِيَّةِ وَالْوَقْفِيَّةِ وَغَيْرِهَا مِنَ الْحُقُوقِ الْعَيْنِيَّةِ فِي الْعَقَارَاتِ الْمُسَجَّلَةِ فِيهِ .

(2) تَرَى هَذِهِ الْمَوَاطِنَ الَّتِي قَبِلُوا فِيهَا شَهَادَةَ التَّسَامُعِ فِي كِتَابِي «أَحْكَامِ الْأَوْقَافِ» ف/ ١٣٤، وَفِي شُرُوحِ الْمَادَّةِ / ١٦٨٨ / مِنَ الْمَجَلَّةِ، وَفِي «رَدِّ الْمُحْتَارِ» لِابْنِ عَابِدِينَ ٤/ ١٣٣

ثُمَّ الْبَابُ الثَّانِي: مَصَادِرُ الْفِقْهِ الْإِسْلَامِيِّ

أَدَاؤُهَا وَلَيْسَ لَهُمْ دَلِيلٌ أَوْ تَعْلِيلٌ فِي هَذَا التَّخْصِيصِ إِلَّا الْمَصْلَحَةُ الْمُرْسَلَةُ .

٢ - وَرَدَ أَيْضًا فِي الْحَدِيثِ النَّبَوِيِّ الثَّابِتِ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ نَهَى عَنْ بَيْعِ الْإِنْسَانِ مَا لَيْسَ عِنْدَهُ (١) ، وَهِيَ قَضِيَّةُ الْمَعْدُومِ؛ كَمَا نَهَى أَيْضًا عَنْ بَيْعِ الْغَرَرِ (٢) ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ بَيَانُ مَعْنَاهُ: (ر: ف ٢٠/٥ الْحَاشِيَةِ).

لَكِنَّ فُقَهَاءَ الْمَذْهَبِ الْحَنَفِيِّ خَصَّصُوا هَذَيْنِ الْحَدِيثَيْنِ، فَجَوَّزُوا الْمَوَاسِمَ الثَّمَرِيَّةَ فِي الْكُرُومِ وَسَائِرِ الْأَشْجَارِ ذَاتِ الثِّمَارِ الْمُتَلَاحِقَةِ (٣)، مَتَى ظَهَرَ بَعْضُ هَذِهِ الثِّمَارِ فَقَطْ وَبَدَا صَلَاحُهَا؛ وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الْمَصْلَحَةَ تَقْضِي بِتَجْوِيزِ هَذَا الْبَيْعِ لِحَاجَةِ النَّاسِ إِلَيْهِ .
وَفِي هَذَا التَّجْوِيزِ تَخْصِيصٌ لِلْحَدِيثَيْنِ الْمَذْكُورَيْنِ، فَإِنَّ الثِّمَارَ الَّتِي سَتُوجَدُ مَعْدُومَةٌ وَقْتَ الْبَيْعِ، فَفِيهَا بَيْعٌ لِمَعْدُومٍ؛ كَمَا أَنَّ كَمِّيَّتَهَا احْتِمَالِيَّةٌ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَحْدِيدُهَا وَقْتَ الْبَيْعِ؛ فَفِيهَا نَوْعٌ مِنَ الْغَرَرِ .

٣ - قَرَّرَ فُقَهَاءُ الْمَذْهَبِ الْحَنَفِيِّ قَبُولَ شَهَادَةِ النِّسَاءِ وَحْدَهُنَّ فِيمَا لَا يَطَّلِعُ عَلَيْهِ إِلَّا النِّسَاءُ فَقَطْ، كَالْجَرَائِمِ الَّتِي تَقَعُ فِي حَمَّامَاتِهِنَّ، وَكَشَهَادَةِ الْقَابِلَةِ عَلَى الْوِلَادَةِ وَتَعْيِينِ الْوَلَدِ عِنْدَ النِّزَاعِ فِيهِ .
وَبِذَلِكَ قَدْ خَصَّصُوا نُصُوصَ الْقُرْآنِ وَالسُّنَّةِ الَّتِي تَشْتَرِطُ فِي الشَّهَادَةِ عُنْصُرَ الذُّكُورَةِ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ شُهُودُ الْإِثْبَاتِ رِجَالًا فَقَطْ، أَوْ رِجَالًا وَنِسَاءً مَعًا .
وَهَذَا التَّخْصِيصُ أَيْضًا بِمُقْتَضَى الْمَصْلَحَةِ، إِذْ بِدُونِهِ تَضِيعُ هَذِهِ الْحُقُوقُ.

(١) عَنْ حَكِيمِ بْنِ حِزَامٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ قَالَ لَهُ: «لَا تَبِعْ مَا لَيْسَ عِنْدَكَ» رَوَاهُ أَبُو دَاوُدَ (٣٥٠٣) وَالتِّرْمِذِيُّ (١٢٣٢) وَقَالَ: حَسَنٌ صَحِيحٌ، وَالنَّسَائِيُّ (٤٦١٣) . وَقَالَ ابْنُ دَقِيقِ الْعِيدِ فِي آخِرِ «الِاقْتِرَاحِ» :٣٠٣ وَهُوَ عَلَى شَرْطِ الشَّيْخَيْنِ (ر: ف ٣٧٢).

(٢) رَوَاهُ مُسْلِمٌ (١٥١٣) فِي الْبُيُوعِ، وَالتِّرْمِذِيُّ (١٢٣٠)، وَأَبُو دَاوُدَ (٣٣٧٦)، وَالنَّسَائِيُّ (٤٥١٨).

(٣) الْمُرَادُ بِالْمُتَلَاحِقَةِ الَّتِي لَا تَنْعَقِدُ عَلَى أَشْجَارِهَا دُفْعَةً وَاحِدَةً بَلْ كُلَّمَا قُطِفَتْ أَخْلَفَتْ، كَالْعِنَبِ وَكَالْخُرْشُوفِ الْمُسَمَّى فِي الْبِلَادِ الشَّامِيَّةِ الْيَوْمَ (أَنْكَنَار) أَوْ (شَوْكِي).
Chapter (5): Subsidiary Sources: Istislah (Consideration of Public Interest)

Generally, it is prohibited for a person to testify before the judiciary about a matter he did not witness but only heard from others.

Based on this, the jurists decided to reject hearsay testimony (shahādat al-tasāmuʿ) in proving rights. However, the Hanafi jurists saw that there are matters in which public interest requires accepting hearsay testimony, because the condition of direct observation is either impossible or not feasible, so this condition would cause the loss of important rights that the Law does not permit neglecting. Thus, they ruled to accept hearsay testimony in numerous matters, including proving the original endowment (waqf): i.e., proving that this property is an endowment and not the property of the possessor when there is a dispute over its endowed or owned status. They justified this by saying that public interest requires accepting hearsay testimony here as istiḥsān (juristic preference) contrary to analogy due to necessity, and that is for the protection of old endowments from loss, because if the endowment becomes ancient and its properties were not registered in the court register, or its register is lost, it becomes impossible to prove its endowment status due to the extinction of witnesses who testified to the wording of the founder when he established the endowment. Thus, every person would be emboldened to seize old endowments and claim ownership of them without the possibility of proving their endowment status if we require direct observation. Therefore, hearsay testimony is accepted in this matter, such that the witness testifies to the endowment based on what he hears from trustworthy people, even if they too are hearsay from similar others and not eyewitnesses. Among the matters in which the Hanafi jurists also accepted hearsay testimony are: proving lineage, death, and consummation of marriage, up to ten places detailed in their books (2). Thus, they made an exception to the text of the Prophetic hadith that requires direct observation for undertaking testimony to be valid.

(1) It is noted here that after the creation of the real estate registry in our countries, its records have become the basis for proving ownership, endowment status, and other real rights in properties registered therein.

(2) You can see these instances where they accepted hearsay testimony in my book 'Ahkām al-Awqāf' p. 134, and in the commentaries on Article 1688 of the Majalla, and in 'Radd al-Muḥtār' by Ibn ʿĀbidīn 4/133.

Then, Part Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence

Their performance and they have no proof or justification for this exception except maslahah mursalah (unrestricted public interest).

2 - It is also reported in the authentic Prophetic hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited a person from selling what he does not possess (1) - this is the case of a non-existent item; and he also prohibited selling gharar (uncertainty) (2), and its meaning has been explained earlier (see: f. 20/5 footnote).

However, the Hanafi school jurists made an exception to these two hadiths, and they permitted seasonal fruit sales in vineyards and all trees bearing sequential fruits (3), once some of these fruits have appeared and their ripeness has become evident; that is because public interest requires permitting this sale due to people's need for it.
And in this permission there is an exception to the two aforementioned hadiths, for the fruits that will exist are non-existent at the time of the sale, so it involves selling a non-existent item; also, their quantity is conjectural and cannot be determined at the time of sale, so it involves a type of gharar.

3 - The Hanafi school jurists ruled to accept the testimony of women alone in matters that only women can observe, such as crimes that occur in their bathhouses, and the testimony of the midwife regarding childbirth and identification of the child in case of dispute.
Thus, they made an exception to the texts of the Quran and Sunnah that require the element of masculinity in testimony, by requiring that witnesses for proof be men only, or men and women together.
This exception is also based on public interest, since without it these rights would be lost.

(1) From Ḥakīm ibn Ḥizām (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to him: 'Do not sell what is not with you.' Narrated by Abū Dāwūd (3503), al-Tirmidhī (1232) and said: hasan sahih, and al-Nasāʾī (4613). Ibn Daqīq al-ʿĪd said in the end of 'al-Iqtirāḥ': p. 303: it is on the condition of the two Shaykhs (see: f. 372).

(2) Narrated by Muslim (1513) in Buyūʿ, al-Tirmidhī (1230), Abū Dāwūd (3376), and al-Nasāʾī (4518).

(3) By sequential fruits are meant those that do not ripen on their trees all at once, but rather whenever they are picked, they are replaced, like grapes and artichokes, which in the Levantine countries are called today 'ankinār' or 'shawkī'.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is from a chapter on subsidiary sources of Islamic law, specifically istiṣlāḥ (consideration of public interest). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains how Hanafi jurists have used unrestricted public interest (maslahah mursalah) to create exceptions to general rules derived from primary texts. The text presents three examples to illustrate this: first, the acceptance of hearsay testimony (shahādat al-tasāmuʿ) in certain matters, particularly for proving old endowments. The Hanafi position is that requiring direct observation (ʿiyān) would lead to the loss of valuable rights, so they allow testimony based on widespread reports (tawātur) from trustworthy sources. This is justified as istiḥsān bi al-ḍarūrah (juristic preference due to necessity). The second example concerns the prohibition of selling what one does not possess and selling gharar (uncertainty). Hanafis permitted the sale of fruit on trees (mawāsim thamariyyah) before full maturity if some fruit has appeared, despite the elements of non-existence and uncertainty at the time of contract. They argued that public need (ḥājah) justifies this exception. The third example is accepting women's testimony alone in matters not normally seen by men, such as childbirth in a women's bathhouse. This makes an exception to the general requirement of male witnesses or mixed male-female witnesses. In all three cases, the only justification is maslahah mursalah – an unrestricted interest that does not contradict any specific textual evidence. The passage shows that Hanafis employ maslahah as a tool for takhṣīṣ (specification) of general proofs when necessary to protect rights and meet needs, but they do not elevate it to an independent source.

Key Terms
شهادة التسامع (shahādat al-tasāmuʿ): Testimony based on hearsay, where the witness did not directly observe the event but heard it from others, even from multiple chains.
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslahah al-mursalah): Unrestricted public interest that is not specifically supported or negated by a textual evidence; used to justify rulings that serve human welfare.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): The method of deriving rulings based on consideration of public interest (maslahah) when no specific textual evidence exists.
التخصيص (al-takhṣīṣ): Specification: narrowing the scope of a general text to exclude certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Hanafi jurists accept hearsay testimony (shahādat al-tasāmuʿ) for proving old endowments?
A) Because direct testimony is always unreliable for old properties.
B) Because the hadith requiring direct witness is weak (ḍaʿīf).
C) Because requiring direct observation would cause loss of endowment rights, and maslahah mursalah justifies the exception.
D) Because all other schools agree with them.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi jurists use maslahah mursalah as a tool to specify general texts when necessary, protecting important rights and meeting public needs.

Hanafi Use of Maslaha Mursala for Takhsis
Demonstrating Hanafi adoption of maslaha mursala with fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of Hanafi takhsis by maslaha mursala and explain how they refute the claim that Hanafis do not use this source.

Core Idea
Hanafis specify general texts by public need even when no explicit evidence exists.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' addresses a common misconception: that the Hanafi school does not employ maslaha mursala (unrestricted public interest) as a source for specifying legal texts. Al-Zarqa provides two concrete fiqh examples from Hanafi jurisprudence that can only be explained by resort to maslaha, demonstrating that Hanafis do indeed practice takhsis by maslaha, albeit often under the name of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two hadiths that are specified
The passage cites the Prophet's prohibitions of selling what one does not possess (bay' ma laysa 'indaka) and selling gharar (uncertainty). These are general texts that would normally prohibit certain transactions.
Both hadiths are established in Sunni hadith collections.
2
Analyze the first Hanafi specification: advance fruit sales
Hanafi jurists permitted the seasonal advance sale (mawasim) of fruits on vines and trees once some fruit appears and shows ripeness, despite involving selling non-existent items and uncertainty. The justification given is public need (maslaha).
This is a specification (takhsis) of the two hadiths.
3
Analyze the second specification: women's testimony
Hanafi jurists accepted the sole testimony of women in matters only women can witness, such as crimes in women's baths or midwife testimony on childbirth, despite Quranic and Sunnah texts requiring male witnesses or a mixed group. The basis is maslaha to preserve rights.
Rights preservation is one of the five essential necessities (daruriyyat).
4
Understand the author's conclusion
Al-Zarqa explicitly states that these examples prove Hanafis adopt takhsis by maslaha mursala, contrary to Muhammad Abu Zahra's claim that Hanafis differ from Malikis on this point.
The author considers this a refutation based on evidence from Hanafi fiqh.
5
Evaluate the significance for iftaa
Recognizing Hanafi use of maslaha mursala expands the tools available for fatwa, allowing flexibility in novel situations without explicit textual evidence.
This supports the idea that Hanafi jurisprudence is not rigid but adapts to needs.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the use of maslaha mursala (unrestricted public interest) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law, specifically within the Hanafi school. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents evidence that Hanafi jurists do utilize maslaha mursala to specify (takhsis) general textual evidence, contrary to a common misconception that only Malikis employ it. The author provides two concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh. First, despite the Prophet's prohibition of selling what one does not possess (bay' ma laysa 'indaka) and selling gharar (uncertainty), Hanafi jurists permitted the advance sale of seasonal fruits (mawasim thamariyya) on vines and other trees with successive crops once some fruit appears and shows ripeness. This permission, justified by public need, involves selling non-existent items and uncertainty, thus specifying the hadiths. Second, Hanafi jurists accepted the testimony of women alone in matters exclusively known to women, such as crimes in women's baths or midwife testimony on childbirth, despite Quranic and Sunnah texts requiring male witnesses or a combination. This specification is also based on maslaha to protect rights, which is one of the five essential necessities. The author then explicitly states that these examples show Hanafis adopt takhsis by maslaha mursala, refuting the view of Muhammad Abu Zahra. The passage concludes by emphasizing the importance of studying istihsan and maslaha mursala thoroughly in modern times to harness the flexibility of Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
المصلحة المرسلة (al-maslaha al-mursala): Unrestricted public interest that is not explicitly recognized or rejected by a specific textual evidence, used as a basis for legal rulings especially when necessity arises.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): The act of specifying a general textual evidence (amm) to exclude some of its individual cases, often based on another evidence or maslaha.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): The method of deriving rulings based on maslaha mursala; synonymous with the application of maslaha mursala.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Prohibiting all forms of advance sales because they involve uncertainty.
D) Accepting women's testimony only in financial transactions.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis do use maslaha mursala to specify general texts, as shown by their rulings on advance fruit sales and women's sole testimony, contrary to the claim that only Malikis employ this source.

Hanafi Adoption of Maslahah Mursalah via Istihsan
Argues that Hanafi school adopts maslahah mursalah via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school incorporates maslahah mursalah through istihsan and evaluate the claim that Hanafis reject it.

Core Idea
Hanafis adopt maslahah mursalah not independently but through istihsan.

Many students of fiqh assume that maslahah mursalah is exclusively a Maliki tool. In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa challenges that view, showing that Hanafi jurists also employ unrestricted public interest—but through the principle of istihsan. This lesson unpacks al-Zarqa's evidence and explains the mechanism by which Hanafis accommodate maslahah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Note that al-Zarqa opposes Muhammad Abu Zahra's assertion that Hanafis differ from Malikis in rejecting maslahah mursalah.
The refutation is based on evidence from Hanafi fiqh.
2
Locate the Hanafi mechanism
Al-Zarqa points to istihsan as the vehicle for maslahah. Understand that istihsan allows departure from qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or public interest.
Istihsan is a recognised Hanafi source (dalil) even if maslahah is not formalised.
3
Analyse the examples (implied)
The passage references earlier examples (shawahid) that demonstrate Hanafi use of maslahah. If you have access to the preceding sections, study those cases.
E.g., the validity of al-istisna (contract of manufacture) is based on istihsan/maslahah.
4
Evaluate the implications for usul al-fiqh
Understand that the debate is not just terminological but affects how we view the flexibility of Hanafi methodology.
The mufta bihi position is that maslahah is considered, but always within the framework of nusus.

Siyasah Shar'iyyah: Definition and Relation to Istislah
Defines siyasah shar'iyyah and shows its relation to istislah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-siyasah al-shar'iyyah and explain its relationship to istislah, distinguishing its scope including civil measures.

Core Idea
Siyasah shar'iyyah lets the ruler promote good and prevent evil, as long as no text is contradicted.

This passage introduces the concept of siyasah shar'iyyah as a tool for the ruler to enact measures that serve public interest within Shariah boundaries. It clarifies that such measures are a subset of istislah and can include both physical punishments and civil sanctions like voiding contracts. Understanding this concept is essential for muftis when issuing fatwas on state policy and ta'zir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition Components
Extract the four components of the definition: agent (ruler), purpose (righteousness, avoid corruption), limit (not contradicting Shariah), and that it is a type of istislah.
The definition is attributed to Hanbali jurist Ibn 'Aqil, but widely accepted.
2
Recognize the Example: Ta'zir
Note that ta'zir (discretionary punishments) is given as a famous example. Understand that ta'zir is left to the ruler's judgment, unlike fixed hudud.
Hanafis allow ta'zir for offenses not covered by hudud.
3
Understand the Relationship to Istislah
The author states siyasah shar'iyyah is a type of istislah. Reflect on how istislah (consideration of public interest) encompasses such discretionary policies.
4
Expand Scope: Civil Sanctions
Recognize that deterrent measures include civil actions like voiding contracts, not just physical punishment. This broadens the application of siyasah.
The passage gives an example of marriage during 'iddah being void.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a ruler's action to curb misuse of a permissible right by voiding a contract. Determine if it qualifies as siyasah shar'iyyah.
Such an action is valid if it meets the conditions (purpose, no text contradiction).

الاستصلاح والسياسة الشرعية

Supplement to Chapter Five:
al-Istislah and al-Siyasah al-Shar'iyyah.
26/5 - Al-Siyasah al-Shar'iyyah is an action by the ruler that brings people closer to righteousness and farther from corruption, even if the Messenger did not do it nor was revelation sent down concerning it, as long as it does not contradict what the Shariah has stated (1), i.e., it is permissible. Among the most famous examples of siyasah shar'iyyah mentioned by jurists are al-ta'zirat (singular: ta'zir), which are punishments whose determination the Shariah has left to the ruler.
In the terminology of the jurists, the term siyasah shar'iyyah is predominantly used to refer to such deterrent measures that prevent people from misusing legal rulings and permissible matters.
It is easy to notice that siyasah shar'iyyah is a type of istislah (consideration of public interest). This is confirmed when we remember that the deterrent measures that siyasah shar'iyyah may involve are not limited to physical punishments, but may also include civil sanctions that nullify a contract or transaction, thereby preventing the offender from achieving his aim. This is illustrated in the following two examples:
:
27/5 - Marrying a woman during her 'iddah period – the Great Qur'an has prohibited marriage (3) marriage.
(1) This is the definition of the Hanbali jurist Ibn 'Aqil. See: Shari'at al-Islam by Dr. Yusuf al-Qaradawi, p. 25, and al-Turuq al-Hukmiyyah fi al-Siyasah al-Shar'iyyah by Ibn Qayyim, pp. 15-17.
(2) Verses 234 and 235 of Surah al-Baqarah.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines siyasah shar'iyyah (Shariah-oriented policy) as discretionary actions by the ruler that aim to promote good and prevent evil, as long as they do not contradict explicit Shariah texts. The definition is attributed to the Hanbali jurist Ibn 'Aqil. The author then gives ta'zir (discretionary punishments) as a key example. He emphasizes that siyasah shar'iyyah falls under the broader category of istislah (public interest). Importantly, he notes that deterrent measures can be civil (e.g., voiding a contract) rather than just physical punishment. The incomplete example of marriage during 'iddah (prohibited by Quran 2:234-235) would illustrate a civil sanction: such a marriage is void, preventing the violator from achieving his aim. Thus, siyasah shar'iyyah is a flexible tool for the ruler within the framework of Shariah.

Key Terms
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyasah al-shar'iyyah): Actions by the ruler that bring people closer to righteousness and further from corruption, without contradicting Shariah.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishments left to the ruler's judgment, not prescribed in fixed texts.

Siyasah shar'iyyah is a legitimate tool for rulers to promote public interest within Shariah, encompassing both penal and civil measures.

Siyasa Shar'iyya: Definition and Application
Defining siyasa shar'iyya and illustrating with ta'zir and marriage during iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define siyasa shar'iyya, distinguish it from istislah, and apply it to the example of marriage during iddah.

Core Idea
Siyasa shar'iyya allows rulers to deter misuse of Shari'ah through discretionary penalties, provided no explicit text is contradicted.

This passage introduces the concept of siyasa shar'iyya (Shari'ah-oriented policy), a discretionary tool for rulers to promote public welfare. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, defines it using the Hanbali jurist Ibn 'Aqil and explains that it falls under the broader principle of istislah (public interest). The key limitation: siyasa cannot contradict explicit Quran or Sunnah. The classic example given is Caliph 'Umar's ruling on marriage during 'iddah, where he imposed a permanent prohibition as a deterrent, even though the Quran only prohibits the marriage without specifying a worldly penalty.

Guided Path
1
Define siyasa shar'iyya
Extract the definition from the passage: it is the ruler's action that brings people closer to righteousness and away from corruption, as long as it does not contradict Shari'ah.
The definition is from Ibn 'Aqil (Hanbali) but widely accepted.
2
Identify its key condition
The defining condition: siyasa must not violate any explicit Shari'ah text. This distinguishes it from arbitrary rule.
The passage uses the phrase 'as long as it does not contradict what the Shari'ah has explicitly stated'.
3
Relate siyasa to istislah
The author states that siyasa is a type of istislah (public interest). Recognise that istislah is a broader concept; siyasa is its application by the ruler.
Hanafis do not formally use istislah as a source, but they accept siyasa under ta'zir and siyar.
4
Analyse the example: marriage during iddah
The Quran prohibits marriage during iddah but does not set a penalty. 'Umar annulled the marriage and permanently barred the man from marrying her. This is a siyasa measure because it deters misuse of the ruling and does not contradict any text (the text only prohibits the marriage, not a permanent bar).
Imams Malik and Ahmad adopted this ijtihad; Hanafis have a different view (no permanent prohibition).
5
Generalise the application
Siyasa measures can be penal (ta'zir) or civil (annulment of contracts). They are discretionary but must aim at public welfare.
The passage mentions that ta'zir is the most famous example of siyasa.

الباب الثاني: مصادر الفقه الإسلامي
بامرأة خلال عدتها وقد حصل في خلافة عمر رضي الله عنه أنَّ رجلاً تزوج امرأة في عدتها، ففرق بينهما عمر ومنع الرجل أن يتزوج بها أبداً أي) حتى بعد انقضاء عدتها). وقد أخذ الإمامان مالك وأحمد بهذا. الاجتهاد فقالا بأن من تزوج امرأة في عدتها يمنع من الزواج بها أبداً(۱) فهذا المنع المؤبد يعني إبطال عقد الزواج عقوبة لهذه المخالفة، وهي عقوبة قدرت بالاجتهاد وعلى أساس السياسة الشرعية، إذ لم ينص القرآن العظيم على عقوبة محددة لمن يخالف هذا الحكم.
Appendix to Chapter Five: Istislah and Siyasa Shar'iyya. 26/5 - Siyasa shar'iyya is the action of the ruler by which people are brought closer to righteousness and further from corruption, even if the Messenger did not do it nor was revelation sent down concerning it – as long as it does not contradict what the Shari'ah has explicitly stated (1), i.e., it is permissible. Among the most famous examples of siyasa shar'iyya mentioned by jurists are ta'zir (pl. ta'zirat), which are punishments whose specification the Shari'ah has left to the ruler. In the terminology of jurists, the phrase siyasa shar'iyya is often used to refer to such deterrent measures that prevent people from misusing legal rulings and permissible matters. It is easy to observe that siyasa shar'iyya is a type of istislah, and this is confirmed when we mention that deterrent measures which siyasa shar'iyya may involve are not limited to physical punishments, but may be civil sanctions that annul a contract or an action, thereby preventing the perpetrator from achieving his aim. This is clarified in the following two examples: 27/5 - Marriage to a woman during her 'iddah: the Great Quran (2) prohibits marriage to a woman during her 'iddah. It occurred during the caliphate of 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, that a man married a woman during her 'iddah, so 'Umar separated them and permanently prevented the man from marrying her ever (i.e., even after the end of her 'iddah). The two Imams Malik and Ahmad adopted this ijtihad and said that whoever marries a woman during her 'iddah is permanently prevented from marrying her. This permanent prohibition means annulment of the marriage contract as a punishment for this violation, and it is a punishment determined by ijtihad based on siyasa shar'iyya, since the Great Quran did not specify a fixed punishment for whoever violates this ruling.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines siyasa shar'iyya (legal policy) as discretionary measures taken by the ruler to promote public welfare, provided they do not contradict explicit Shari'ah texts. The definition is attributed to the Hanbali jurist Ibn 'Aqil. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that siyasa shar'iyya falls under the broader concept of istislah (public interest). The most common example is ta'zir (discretionary punishment). Importantly, the deterrent measures of siyasa are not limited to corporal penalties; they can include civil sanctions like contract annulment. The first example given is marriage during 'iddah. The Quran prohibits it but does not prescribe a worldly penalty. Caliph 'Umar, exercising siyasa, ruled that the marriage is annulled and the man is permanently barred from marrying that woman. Imams Malik and Ahmad adopted this view. This illustrates how the ruler can establish penalties through ijtihad based on siyasa shar'iyya.

Key Terms
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyāsa al-sharʿiyya): Ruler's discretionary actions that promote public welfare without contradicting Shari'ah texts.
التعزير (al-taʿzīr): Discretionary punishments left to the ruler's judgment for offenses without fixed hadd penalties.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Basis of rulings derived from public interest, broader than siyasa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Umar's ruling on marriage during 'iddah?
A) A Quranic verse specifying the penalty.
B) Ijtihad based on siyasa shar'iyya.
C) Consensus of all companions.
D) A hadith from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Siyasa shar'iyya is a ruler's discretionary policy to promote welfare, subject to non-contradiction of Shari'ah texts.

Siyasa Shar'iyya: 'Umar's Ruling on Marriage during 'Iddah
Historical example of siyasa shar'iyya by Caliph 'Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Caliph 'Umar's ruling on marriage during 'iddah exemplifies siyasa shar'iyya and distinguish the Hanafi/Shafi'i position from the Maliki/Hanbali position on the perpetual prohibition.

Core Idea
Siyasa shar'iyya permits a ruler to impose a proportionate penalty when the Quran is silent, even if other mujtahids differ.

This lesson explores a landmark ruling by Caliph 'Umar: a man who married a woman during her 'iddah was permanently barred from marrying her. The Quran specified no penalty for this violation, so 'Umar used ijtihad and siyasa shar'iyya to deter future abuse. We will analyse the ruling, the inter-madhhab dispute it generated, and what this teaches us about the nature of siyasa shar'iyya as a legitimate source of law in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What exactly did 'Umar rule? He separated the couple and permanently prohibited the man from marrying the woman—even after her 'iddah ended.
This goes beyond the normal rule that marriage becomes permissible after 'iddah.
2
Understand the basis: siyasa shar'iyya
Why did 'Umar make this ruling? The Quran forbids marriage during 'iddah but prescribes no penalty. 'Umar's ruling is an ijtihad-based penalty (ta'zir) to protect the sanctity of 'iddah and deter violation.
This is a classic case of siyasa shar'iyya: ruling by a ruler based on public interest.
3
Identify the opposing ijtihad
The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools hold that the man may marry the woman after her 'iddah ends—no perpetual prohibition. They rely on general permissibility and the absence of a textual basis for permanent ban.
Note: The text does not provide their detailed proof, but the footnote refers to al-Jassas.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Maliki and Hanbali courts would invalidate any later marriage between them, while Hanafi and Shafi'i courts would permit it after 'iddah.
This affects rights and obligations of the parties.
5
Extract the principle of siyasa shar'iyya
A ruler may, by ijtihad, impose a penalty or modify a ruling when the primary texts are silent and the public interest demands it. This is bounded by the objectives of Shari'ah and the ruler's authority.
Siyasa shar'iyya is not arbitrary; it must be grounded in maslahah and not contradict a definitive text.

Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence
A woman during her 'iddah: It occurred during the caliphate of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that a man married a woman during her 'iddah. 'Umar separated them and prevented the man from ever marrying her (i.e., even after the expiry of her 'iddah). Imams Malik and Ahmad adopted this ijtihad, saying that whoever marries a woman during her 'iddah is permanently prohibited from marrying her (1). This permanent prohibition means nullifying the marriage contract as a punishment for this violation, a punishment determined by ijtihad and on the basis of siyasa shar'iyya, since the Great Quran did not prescribe a specific penalty for violating this ruling.
28/5 - Talaq al-Firar: The Hanafi jurists use this term to denote a man divorcing his wife with an irrevocable divorce without her consent while he is terminally ill. This is an abuse of the right to divorce, because it constitutes a departure from the purpose for which divorce was legislated, namely relief from marital discord, as the Hanafi jurists say. When a man is terminally ill or in a similar situation, such as facing an accident likely to cause destruction, or being led to execution for retaliation, he is headed for eternal relief from the marital discord he claims to suffer, so there remains no explanation for his divorcing his wife in this situation except one: he intends divorce as revenge and harm to deprive her of inheritance, not as relief from marital discord. This is clear abuse.
And Sayyiduna 'Uthman ruled that the divorced woman inherits from her husband in such a case (2), contrary to the normal situation where spouses do not inherit from each other after an irrevocable divorce.
(1) The Hanafi and Shafi'i ijtihads are to the contrary. See Ahkam al-Quran by Abu Bakr al-Jassas, in his commentary on the two aforementioned noble verses.
(2) It seems that this is also the ijtihad of Sayyiduna 'Ali (may Allah honor his face). The three schools adopted this ijtihad, restricting it with additional conditions, while the Shafi'is differed and did not allow inheritance for the woman divorced in such a manner. For details, see: Malik by Abu Zahra, p. 198, and Bidayat al-Mujtahid by Ibn Rushd (2/68, Cairo, al-Khanji edition, 1329 AH).
Appendix to Chapter (5): Istislah and Siyasa Shar'iyya
Thus, making the wife inherit from her husband after his divorce of her is an annulment of one of the effects of divorce when it is strongly probable that the husband misused divorce for other than its intended purpose, so his evil intent is countered. This is from siyasa shar'iyya.
And given the close connection between istislah and siyasa shar'iyya, the examples of one are often examples of the other. We will mention, when discussing the sixth stage of fiqh in the appendix to Chapter 15, examples of istislih rulings issued by sultanic orders at that time which annul some transactions or restrict some ijtihad rulings. We will also transmit in the first appendix to Chapter 22 valuable words by Ibn al-Qayyim about types of istislahi regulations and orders he called "political laws" (see footnote 9/22 below).
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition presenting two historical examples of siyasa shar'iyya (Islamic governance policy) rulings. The first example: Caliph 'Umar's ruling that a man who marries a woman during her 'iddah is permanently prohibited from marrying her, even after the 'iddah ends. This was an ijtihad based on public interest, as the Quran specified no penalty. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ, allowing marriage after the 'iddah. The second example: talaq al-firar, where a terminally ill man divorces his wife to deprive her of inheritance. Hanafi jurists see this as an abuse of the right to divorce. Caliph 'Uthman (and 'Ali) ruled that the divorced woman inherits from her husband in such cases, overturning the normal rule that irrevocable divorce cuts inheritance. The three schools (Hanafi, Maliki, Hanbali) adopted this with conditions, while Shafi'is did not. The author ties these to siyasa shar'iyya: rulers may counter misuse of legal rights by modifying effects based on strong probability and public interest. The close link between istislah and siyasa shar'iyya is noted, with promise of further examples.

Key Terms
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyasa al-shar'iyya): Rulings or policies issued by a ruler based on ijtihad and public interest, within the framework of Shari'ah, to address situations where no specific textual ruling exists or to counter misuse of rights.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Seeking the public interest (maslahah) as a basis for legal rulings when there is no specific text or consensus.
الإجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning by a qualified jurist to derive a ruling from the sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Caliph 'Umar's ruling of permanent prohibition on marriage during 'iddah?
A) A direct Quranic verse commanding permanent prohibition.
B) A hadith from the Prophet ﷺ specifying this penalty.
C) Ijtihad and siyasa shar'iyya because the Quran did not specify a penalty.
D) The consensus of all Companions.

Key Takeaway
Caliph 'Umar's ruling illustrates how a ruler may use siyasa shar'iyya to impose a proportionate penalty when primary texts are silent, even if it disagrees with other valid ijtihads.

The Relationship Between Istiṣlāḥ and Siyāsah Shar‘iyyah
Conceptual link between istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the close relationship between istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah and identify how they are applied in a specific Hanafi legal example.

Core Idea
Istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah are so closely linked that their examples are often interchangeable.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains the intimate connection between two key concepts in Islamic legal theory: istiṣlāḥ (public interest) and siyāsah shar‘iyyah (Shariah-governed policy). He uses the example of a husband who divorces his wife to deprive her of inheritance, and the ruler's intervention to restore her right, illustrating how siyāsah shar‘iyyah can override a normal legal effect to serve maṣlaḥah. This lesson will help you understand how these concepts interact in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key concepts
Define istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah based on the key terms provided. Note their definitions and how they relate to public welfare within Shariah boundaries.
2
Analyze the given example
The passage discusses a husband who divorces his wife to cut off her inheritance. Why does the ruler intervene? What principle is being applied? Understand that the ruler's action negates the effect of divorce due to the husband's evil intention.
This is an application of siyāsah shar‘iyyah to prevent misuse of the law.
3
Recognize the close link
The author explicitly states that due to the close connection, examples of one often serve as examples of the other. This means that many cases can be analyzed under both concepts simultaneously.
They are not mutually exclusive; they overlap significantly.
4
Note the references to further examples
The author mentions upcoming appendices that will provide more examples of istiṣlāḥ rulings issued by sultanic orders, as well as Ibn Qayyim's discussion of 'political laws.' This indicates the breadth of the concept.
These references show that the relationship is well-recognized in Islamic legal literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملحق بالفصل (٥) الاستصلاح والسياسة الشرعية
فتوريث الزوجة من زوجها بعد طلاقها منه هو إبطال لأحد آثار الطلاق، حين يغلب على الظن بأنَّ الزوج أساء باستخدامه الطلاق لغير ما شرع له، فرد عليه قصده السيء، وهذا من السياسة الشرعية.
ونظراً للصلة الوثيقة بين الاستصلاح والسياسة الشرعية فإن الأمثلة على أحدهما تصلح غالباً أمثلة على الآخر. وسنورد عند الحديث عن الدور السادس من أدوار الفقه في الملحق بالفصل ١٥ القادم) أمثلة على أحكام استصلاحية صدرت بأوامر سلطانية في ذلك الحين تبطل بعض التصرفات، أو تقيّد بعض الأحكام الاجتهادية. كما سننقل في الملحق الأول بالفصل الثاني والعشرين كلاماً نفيساً لابن القيم عن أنواع من التنظيمات والأوامر الاستصلاحية سماها : القوانين السياسية» (ر : حاشية ف ٩/٢٢ الآتية).
Appendix to Chapter (5): Istiṣlāḥ and Siyāsah Shar‘iyyah. Making the wife inherit from her husband after his divorce of her is an annulment of one of the effects of divorce, when it is strongly presumed that the husband misused divorce for purposes other than that for which it was legislated, so his evil intention is rejected, and this is from siyāsah shar‘iyyah. And due to the close connection between istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah, the examples of one are often suitable as examples of the other. We will mention, when discussing the sixth period of fiqh in the appendix to Chapter 15 (to come), examples of istiṣlāḥ rulings issued by sultanic orders at that time that invalidate certain transactions or restrict certain ijtihādī rulings. And we will also quote in the first appendix to Chapter 22 a valuable statement by Ibn Qayyim about types of regulations and istiṣlāḥ orders he called 'political laws' (see footnote 9/22 below).
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an appendix to Chapter 5 discussing the concepts of istiṣlāḥ (public interest) and siyāsah shar‘iyyah (Shariah-oriented policy). It gives an example from family law: disallowing a husband's misuse of divorce by making the wife inherit, which negates the effect of divorce. It emphasizes the close relationship between the two concepts, noting that examples of one often apply to the other. The author references upcoming appendices for further examples, including sultanic orders that nullify transactions or restrict ijtihād, and Ibn Qayyim's discussion of 'political laws'.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): A legal method that bases rulings on consideration of public interest (maṣlaḥah) not explicitly addressed by primary sources.
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyāsah al-shar‘iyyah): Governance and administration in accordance with Shariah, allowing rulers to issue regulations for public welfare within Shariah boundaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah?
A) They are completely separate concepts with no overlap.
B) They are closely related; examples of one often serve as examples of the other.
C) Istiṣlāḥ is a subset of siyāsah shar‘iyyah.
D) Siyāsah shar‘iyyah is always based on istiṣlāḥ.

Key Takeaway
Istiṣlāḥ and siyāsah shar‘iyyah are deeply interconnected; rulings based on public interest can be implemented through state policy, and examples of one often serve as examples of the other.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Definition and Conditions of Custom ('Urf) as a Subsidiary Source
Introduces 'urf, its linguistic and terminological meaning, conditions, and maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'urf, state its conditions for legal validity, and apply the maxim 'Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant' to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Custom is a subsidiary source only if it is consistent or predominant, not merely widespread.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of 'urf (custom) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law. Understanding when a custom becomes legally binding is crucial for a mufti, as many daily transactions rely on customary practices. The Hanafi school has developed precise conditions to distinguish between a valid custom and mere individual behavior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'urf
First, understand the linguistic and terminological meaning of 'urf from the passage. Linguistically, it means 'known' or 'familiar'; terminologically, it is the custom of the majority of a people in a saying or action.
2
Understand the conditions for validity
The passage states that 'urf is only considered if it is consistent (mutṭarid) or predominant (ghālib). Consistency means it is always observed; predominance means it is observed by the vast majority.
If a practice is only done by a few or varies, it is not a valid custom.
3
Apply the condition to an example
Consider the example of mahr division in the Levant: two-thirds immediate, one-third deferred. If this practice is consistent or predominant, it is a valid custom. Otherwise, it is an individual arrangement.
4
Learn and use the maxim
The maxim 'إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطردت أو غلبت' (Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant) is the key takeaway. Memorize it and be able to apply it.
This maxim is from the Majallah, article 41.
