Chapter Six: Subsidiary Sources: Custom ('Urf)
1/6 - Meaning of 'Urf Linguistically and Terminologically:
'Urf, with a damma on the 'ayn, originally in language means knowledge. Then it was used linguistically to mean the known, familiar, approved thing that sound minds accept.
And accordingly, Allah Almighty said: "Take the forgiveness and command what is customary ('urf) and turn away from the ignorant" (Surah al-A'raf, 7:199).
In juristic terminology, 'urf is: the custom of the majority of a people in a saying or action.
(1) The meaning of 'afw in the original language is the intent to take something... etc.
(2) We derived this definition from several definitions of the jurists mentioned by Ibn 'Abidin in his treatise...
Section Two: Sources of Islamic Fiqh
Hence it is also called: 'adah (custom) and ta'amul (common practice).
From this definition it is understood that 'urf in a matter is not realized unless it is consistent among the people in the place where it is practiced, or predominant, such that most of the people of this 'urf each observe it and act according to it; like, for example, the common practice in the Levant that the dower (mahr) named for the woman in the marriage contract is two-thirds immediate and one-third deferred until after death or divorce. So for the formation of 'urf, a shared habit among the majority must be realized, and this only occurs in the case of consistency or at least predominance; otherwise it is an individual action, not 'urf.
And on this, the jurists laid down the maxim: "Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant" (Majallah, article 41).
2/6 - The Status of Custom Among the Sources of Rulings.
In the social life of people who have no divine law, customs and traditions are the law to which they refer.
Hence we know the ancient authority of custom among humans.
And since some customs may be in themselves good and just, or ugly and unjust, it was among the objectives of revealed laws to approve good custom and forbid the ugly.
(1) Jurists of positive law count five sources... etc. Among these five sources, positive rulings are derived... This has two advantages: first, people have prior knowledge in their transactions, so their disputes decrease. Because people's ignorance of rulings generates problems among them.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage introduces 'urf (custom) as a subsidiary source of Islamic jurisprudence. It begins by explaining the linguistic meaning of 'urf, which originally means knowledge, and then evolved to mean that which is known and approved by sound minds. The author cites Qur'an 7:199 to support the linguistic usage. The terminological definition is given as 'the custom of the majority of a people in a saying or action,' derived from multiple jurists including Ibn 'Abidin. The passage emphasizes that for custom to be legally recognized, it must be either consistent (mutṭarid) or predominant (ghālib) among the people in a given place. This is the condition for its validity. An example is given from the Levant regarding the division of mahr (dower) into immediate and deferred portions. If a practice is not consistent or predominant, it is merely an individual action and not a valid custom. The author then presents the well-known maxim from the Majallah: 'Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant.' The passage concludes by discussing the historical role of custom as a source of law before divine revelation, and notes that Islamic law aims to approve good customs and reject bad ones. The footnote mentions that positive law sources also include custom, but that is outside Islamic fiqh.

Key Terms
العرف (al-‘urf): Custom or common practice of the majority in a saying or action.
عادة (‘ādah): Custom, synonym of 'urf in this context.
اطراد (iṭṭirād): Consistency or prevalence of a practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary condition for a custom ('urf) to be considered a valid source of law in Hanafi fiqh?
A) It must be in writing.
B) It must be consistent or predominant.
C) It must be approved by a mujtahid.
D) It must be practiced by all members of society.

Key Takeaway
'Urf is a subsidiary source in Hanafi fiqh, but it is only valid if it is consistent or predominant and does not contradict a clear text.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Legal Evidence for Custom ('Urf) in Islamic Law
Explaining the two primary textual evidences for 'urf as a subsidiary source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic verse and the athar from Ibn Mas'ud that support 'urf as a subsidiary source of law and explain their evidentiary status in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Custom is validated by the Quranic command to enjoin 'urf and the maxim that what Muslims deem good is good with Allah.

In this lesson, we examine the two primary textual proofs that scholars, especially in the Hanafi school, use to establish the legal authority of 'urf. The first is a verse from Surah al-A'raf, the second is a famous statement from Ibn Mas'ud. Though the verse uses 'urf in its linguistic sense, it provides a strong indication for the technical concept. The athar, though mawquf, is considered a binding proof in legislative matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic proof
Locate the verse from Surah al-A'raf (7:199) quoted in the text: 'Take what is easily given, enjoin what is good, and turn away from the ignorant.' Note the word 'al-'urf' in the verse.
The verse commands 'enjoin what is good'.
2
Analyze the linguistic vs technical meaning
Understand that 'urf in the verse is used in its linguistic sense (what is commonly known and approved), not the technical legal term. Then see how scholars use this as indirect support (istinas) for technical 'urf: because people's customs are what they deem good and need.
The direct meaning is not technical, but the indication is strong.
3
Examine the second proof: the athar from Ibn Mas'ud
Identify the mawquf report: 'What the Muslims consider good is good in the sight of Allah.' Note its classification as mawquf (from a Companion) and its evidentiary value in Hanafi usul.
Mawquf reports on legislative matters are treated like marfu' hadith.
4
Understand why the athar is a proof
Explain that a Companion's statement on legislation cannot be based on personal opinion; thus it carries the weight of Prophetic guidance. The maxim indicates that collective Muslim judgment (which reflects custom) is a valid indicator divine approval.
This supports customs that do not contradict explicit texts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦) المصادر التبعية : العرف
والعرف والعادات إلى اليوم تعد في نظر الحقوقيين مصدراً من أهم المصادر للقوانين الوضعية ذاتها، فيستمد منه واضعوها كثيراً من الأحكام المتعارفة، ويبرزونها في صورة نصوص قانونية يُزال بها الغموض والإبهام الذي لا يجليه العُرف في بعض الحالات.
والشريعة الإسلامية كذلك جاءت فأقرت كثيراً من التصرفات والحقوق المتعارفة بين العرب والإسلام، وهذبت كثيراً ونهت عن كثير، كما أتت بأحكام جديدة استوعبت بها تنظيم الحقوق والالتزامات بين الناس في حياتهم الاجتماعية على أساس وفاء الحاجة والمصلحة والتوجيه إلى أفضل الحلول والنظم لأنَّ الشرائع الإلهية إنما تبغي بأحكامها المدنية تنظيم مصالح البشر وحقوقهم فتقر من متعارف الناس ما تراه محققاً لغايتها، ملائماً لأسسها وأساليبها .
٣/٦ - الدليل الشرعي على اعتبار العرف:
يذكر بعض العلماء الأدلة الشرعية في الإسلام على اعتبار مكانة من العرف الفقهية في إثبات الأحكام قوله تعالى في الآية السالفة البيان: خُذِ الْعَفْوَ وَأْمُرْ بِالْعُرْفِ وَأَعْرِضْ عَنِ الجهلين (١٩٩/٧)
ولا يخفى أنَّ العرف في هذه الآية واقع على معناه اللغوي وهو الأمر المستحسن المألوف، لا على معناه الاصطلاحي الفقهي. ولكن توجيه هذا الاستدلال هو أن العرف في الآية، وإن لم يكن مراداً به المعنى الاصطلاحي، قد يُسْتَأْنَسُ به في تأييد اعتبار العرف بمعناه الاصطلاحي لأنَّ عُرْف الناس في أعمالهم ومعاملاتهم هو مما استحسنوه وألفته عقولهم. والغالب أن عرف القوم دليل على حاجتهم إلى الأمر المتعارف فاعتباره يكون من الأمور المستحسنة.
والثانية - أن تلك الأحكام تكون مألوفة مستساغة ومقبولة، لأنهم قد اعتادوها قبل أن تصبح قانوناً.
ومعظم العلماء يستدلون على مكانة العرف الفقهية في بناء الأحكام الشرعية بأثر قد روي موقوفاً عن عبد الله بن مسعود، وهو من كبار فقهاء صحابة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال: «ما رآه المسلمون حَسَناً فهو عند الله حسن».
٤/٦ - سلطان العرف في الفقه الإسلامي إجمالاً.
إنَّ للعرف في الفقه الإسلامي اعتباراً شرعياً في بناء كثير من أنواع الأحكام التي سنرى بيانها مفصَّلاً في بحثنا عن نظرية العرف في الفقه الإسلامي، آخر القسم الثاني من هذا المدخل الفقهي.
والاجتهادات الفقهية في الإسلام متفقة على هذا الاعتبار للعرف وإن كان بينها شيء من التفاوت في حدوده ومداه.
وقد أقام الفقهاء كبير وزن للعرف في ثبوت الحقوق وانتفائها بين الناس في نواح شتى من المعاملات وضروب التصرفات واعتبروا العرف والعادة أصلاً هاماً ومصدراً عظيماً واسعاً تثبت الأحكام الحقوقية بين الناس على مقتضاه في كل ما لا يصادم نصاً تشريعياً خاصاً يمنعه .
فالعُرف في نظرهم دليل شرعي كاف في ثبوت الأحكام الإلزامية والالتزامات التفصيلية بين الناس حيثما لا دليل سواه؛ بل إنه يترك به.
(١) الأثر الموقوف هو الذي يتكلم به الصحابي دون أن يسنده إلى النبي ﷺ، ومن المقرر أن الأثر الموقوف إذا كان يتضمن أمراً تشريعياً فإنه يعتبر كحديث مروي عن النبي ﷺ لأن الصحابي لا يستطيع أن يقوله برأيه. وبعض الفقهاء يرفع هذا النص إلى النبي ﷺ ولكن التحقيق أنه أثر موقوف على ابن مسعود. وقد رواه الإمام أحمد بن حنبل في مسنده ٣٧٩/١ موقوفاً عليه. (ر: أول رسالة لنشر العرف في بناء بعض الأحكام على العرف لابن عابدين).
Chapter (6) Subsidiary Sources: Custom. And custom and habits to this day are considered in the view of jurists as one of the most important sources for positive laws themselves, so their legislators derive from it many customary rulings, and present them in the form of legal texts by which ambiguity and obscurity that custom does not clarify in some cases are removed. Likewise, Islamic Sharia came and approved many customary practices and rights among the Arabs and Islam, refined many and forbade many, and brought new rulings by which it encompassed the organization of rights and obligations among people in their social life on the basis of fulfilling need and interest and guiding to the best solutions and systems, because divine laws intend by their civil rulings to organize the interests and rights of people, so they approve of what is customary among people that they see as achieving their goal, suitable for their foundations and methods. 3/6 - The Legal Evidence for Considering Custom: Some scholars mention the legal evidences in Islam for the consideration of the juristic status of custom in establishing rulings: His saying, exalted, in the previously mentioned verse: 'Take what is easily given, enjoin what is good, and turn away from the ignorant' (7:199). And it is not hidden that 'urf in this verse is used in its linguistic meaning, which is the commendable and familiar matter, not its juristic technical meaning. But the direction of this inference is that 'urf in the verse, even if it is not intended in the technical sense, may be used as a supporting evidence in affirming the consideration of 'urf in its technical meaning, because people's 'urf in their deeds and transactions is what they have deemed good and their minds have become accustomed to. And generally, the custom of a people is evidence of their need for the customary matter, so its consideration becomes among commendable matters. And the second - that those rulings become familiar, palatable, and accepted, because they have become accustomed to them before they become law. And most scholars infer the juristic status of 'urf in building legal rulings from an athar that has been narrated as mawquf from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, one of the great jurists among the Companions of the Messenger, peace be upon him, that he said: 'What the Muslims consider good is good in the sight of Allah.' 4/6 - The Authority of Custom in Islamic Jurisprudence in Summary: Indeed, custom in Islamic jurisprudence has a legal consideration in building many types of rulings that we will see their detailed explanation in our discussion of the theory of custom in Islamic jurisprudence, at the end of the second part of this juristic introduction. And the juristic ijtihads in Islam are agreed upon this consideration for custom, although there is some variation among them in its limits and scope. And the jurists have given great weight to custom in the establishment and negation of rights among people in various aspects of transactions and types of actions, and they considered custom and habit an important principle and a great, vast source by which legal rulings among people are established according to it in everything that does not conflict with a specific legislative text that prevents it. So custom in their view is a sufficient legal proof in establishing binding rulings and detailed obligations among people wherever there is no other evidence; rather, it is left to it. (1) The mawquf athar is one in which the Companion speaks without attributing it to the Prophet. And it is established that if the mawquf athar contains a legislative matter, it is considered like a hadith narrated from the Prophet because the Companion cannot say it by his own opinion. And some jurists raise this text to the Prophet, but the verified view is that it is a mawquf athar from Ibn Mas'ud. Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated it in his Musnad, 1/379, as mawquf from him. (See: First Treatise on Publishing Custom in Building Some Rulings on Custom by Ibn 'Abidin).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of custom ('urf) as a subsidiary source of Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by noting that even in modern positive law, custom is a major source. Islamic Sharia similarly approved many pre-existing customs, refined some, and prohibited others, while also introducing new rulings. The main focus is the legal evidence for considering custom. Two proofs are presented: first, the Quranic verse from Surah al-A'raf (7:199) which commands 'enjoin what is good' (al-'urf). Though 'urf here is linguistic, scholars use it to support the technical concept since people's customs are what they find good and need. Second, the famous athar from Ibn Mas'ud: 'What the Muslims consider good is good in the sight of Allah.' This is a mawquf report (attributed to a Companion), but it is widely accepted as a proof because a Companion would not say such a thing based on mere opinion. The author then summarizes the authority of custom: it is a recognized source for establishing rights and obligations in transactions and actions, as long as it does not contradict a specific textual ruling. In the Hanafi school, custom is given considerable weight, especially in mu'amalat, and it can even specify general texts or override qiyas in certain cases. The footnote clarifies the nature of mawquf athar and its evidentiary value.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice that people follow and deem good, used as a subsidiary source of law when no explicit text exists.
الأثر الموقوف (al-athar al-mawquf): A statement or action attributed to a Companion, not directly to the Prophet.
ما رآه المسلمون حسنا فهو عند الله حسن (ma ra'ahu al-muslimoon hasanan fahuwa 'inda Allahi hasan): A maxim meaning that the general acceptance of the Muslim community indicates the goodness of a practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a marfu' hadith traced directly to the Prophet.
B) It is a mawquf report from Ibn Mas'ud, but considered legislative and thus probative.
C) It is a weak hadith not used for rulings.
D) It is a Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the Quranic verse and the mawquf athar from Ibn Mas'ud to establish the evidentiary value of custom as a subsidiary source of law.

The Authority and Scope of Custom in Hanafi Fiqh
Summarizing the role and limits of 'urf in Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the authority of custom ('urf) as a subsidiary source and identify its limitations within the school.

Core Idea
Custom is a binding source in mu'amalat except when it conflicts with a specific textual ruling.

Having examined the proofs for 'urf, we now turn to its practical authority and scope within Hanafi jurisprudence. The passage emphasises that 'urf is a fully recognised source for establishing rights and obligations, as long as it does not contradict a specific legislative text. This lesson clarifies the weight given to custom by the Hanafi school and the limits of its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed-upon authority of 'urf
Note the statement that all madhahib agree on considering 'urf as a source, though with variations in limits and scope. Focus on the Hanafi recognition.
Ijma among schools on the principle, not details.
2
Understand the Hanafi reliance on 'urf
The text states that jurists gave great weight to custom in establishing and negating rights, and they considered it a great, vast source. It is a sufficient legal proof in the absence of other evidence.
Custom can even override qiyas in some cases, but not an explicit text.
3
Identify the key limitation of 'urf
The passage explicitly says custom is valid 'in everything that does not conflict with a specific legislative text that prevents it.' This is the crucial limiting condition.
If an explicit text exists, custom cannot overturn it.
4
Recognize the scope of 'urf
The scope covers 'various aspects of transactions and types of actions' (mu'amalat). It does not apply to core ibadat (worship) where texts are detailed.
In Hanafi, 'urf typically operates in mu'amalat and not in fixed acts of worship.

الفصل (٦) المصادر التبعية : العرف
والعرف والعادات إلى اليوم تعد في نظر الحقوقيين مصدراً من أهم المصادر للقوانين الوضعية ذاتها، فيستمد منه واضعوها كثيراً من الأحكام المتعارفة، ويبرزونها في صورة نصوص قانونية يُزال بها الغموض والإبهام الذي لا يجليه العُرف في بعض الحالات.
والشريعة الإسلامية كذلك جاءت فأقرت كثيراً من التصرفات والحقوق المتعارفة بين العرب والإسلام، وهذبت كثيراً ونهت عن كثير، كما أتت بأحكام جديدة استوعبت بها تنظيم الحقوق والالتزامات بين الناس في حياتهم الاجتماعية على أساس وفاء الحاجة والمصلحة والتوجيه إلى أفضل الحلول والنظم لأنَّ الشرائع الإلهية إنما تبغي بأحكامها المدنية تنظيم مصالح البشر وحقوقهم فتقر من متعارف الناس ما تراه محققاً لغايتها، ملائماً لأسسها وأساليبها .
٣/٦ - الدليل الشرعي على اعتبار العرف:
يذكر بعض العلماء الأدلة الشرعية في الإسلام على اعتبار مكانة من العرف الفقهية في إثبات الأحكام قوله تعالى في الآية السالفة البيان: خُذِ الْعَفْوَ وَأْمُرْ بِالْعُرْفِ وَأَعْرِضْ عَنِ الجهلين (١٩٩/٧)
ولا يخفى أنَّ العرف في هذه الآية واقع على معناه اللغوي وهو الأمر المستحسن المألوف، لا على معناه الاصطلاحي الفقهي. ولكن توجيه هذا الاستدلال هو أن العرف في الآية، وإن لم يكن مراداً به المعنى الاصطلاحي، قد يُسْتَأْنَسُ به في تأييد اعتبار العرف بمعناه الاصطلاحي لأنَّ عُرْف الناس في أعمالهم ومعاملاتهم هو مما استحسنوه وألفته عقولهم. والغالب أن عرف القوم دليل على حاجتهم إلى الأمر المتعارف فاعتباره يكون من الأمور المستحسنة.
والثانية - أن تلك الأحكام تكون مألوفة مستساغة ومقبولة، لأنهم قد اعتادوها قبل أن تصبح قانوناً.
ومعظم العلماء يستدلون على مكانة العرف الفقهية في بناء الأحكام الشرعية بأثر قد روي موقوفاً عن عبد الله بن مسعود، وهو من كبار فقهاء صحابة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال: «ما رآه المسلمون حَسَناً فهو عند الله حسن».
٤/٦ - سلطان العرف في الفقه الإسلامي إجمالاً.
إنَّ للعرف في الفقه الإسلامي اعتباراً شرعياً في بناء كثير من أنواع الأحكام التي سنرى بيانها مفصَّلاً في بحثنا عن نظرية العرف في الفقه الإسلامي، آخر القسم الثاني من هذا المدخل الفقهي.
والاجتهادات الفقهية في الإسلام متفقة على هذا الاعتبار للعرف وإن كان بينها شيء من التفاوت في حدوده ومداه.
وقد أقام الفقهاء كبير وزن للعرف في ثبوت الحقوق وانتفائها بين الناس في نواح شتى من المعاملات وضروب التصرفات واعتبروا العرف والعادة أصلاً هاماً ومصدراً عظيماً واسعاً تثبت الأحكام الحقوقية بين الناس على مقتضاه في كل ما لا يصادم نصاً تشريعياً خاصاً يمنعه .
فالعُرف في نظرهم دليل شرعي كاف في ثبوت الأحكام الإلزامية والالتزامات التفصيلية بين الناس حيثما لا دليل سواه؛ بل إنه يترك به.
(١) الأثر الموقوف هو الذي يتكلم به الصحابي دون أن يسنده إلى النبي ﷺ، ومن المقرر أن الأثر الموقوف إذا كان يتضمن أمراً تشريعياً فإنه يعتبر كحديث مروي عن النبي ﷺ لأن الصحابي لا يستطيع أن يقوله برأيه. وبعض الفقهاء يرفع هذا النص إلى النبي ﷺ ولكن التحقيق أنه أثر موقوف على ابن مسعود. وقد رواه الإمام أحمد بن حنبل في مسنده ٣٧٩/١ موقوفاً عليه. (ر: أول رسالة لنشر العرف في بناء بعض الأحكام على العرف لابن عابدين).
Chapter (6) Subsidiary Sources: Custom. And custom and habits to this day are considered in the view of jurists as one of the most important sources for positive laws themselves, so their legislators derive from it many customary rulings, and present them in the form of legal texts by which ambiguity and obscurity that custom does not clarify in some cases are removed. Likewise, Islamic Sharia came and approved many customary practices and rights among the Arabs and Islam, refined many and forbade many, and brought new rulings by which it encompassed the organization of rights and obligations among people in their social life on the basis of fulfilling need and interest and guiding to the best solutions and systems, because divine laws intend by their civil rulings to organize the interests and rights of people, so they approve of what is customary among people that they see as achieving their goal, suitable for their foundations and methods. 3/6 - The Legal Evidence for Considering Custom: Some scholars mention the legal evidences in Islam for the consideration of the juristic status of custom in establishing rulings: His saying, exalted, in the previously mentioned verse: 'Take what is easily given, enjoin what is good, and turn away from the ignorant' (7:199). And it is not hidden that 'urf in this verse is used in its linguistic meaning, which is the commendable and familiar matter, not its juristic technical meaning. But the direction of this inference is that 'urf in the verse, even if it is not intended in the technical sense, may be used as a supporting evidence in affirming the consideration of 'urf in its technical meaning, because people's 'urf in their deeds and transactions is what they have deemed good and their minds have become accustomed to. And generally, the custom of a people is evidence of their need for the customary matter, so its consideration becomes among commendable matters. And the second - that those rulings become familiar, palatable, and accepted, because they have become accustomed to them before they become law. And most scholars infer the juristic status of 'urf in building legal rulings from an athar that has been narrated as mawquf from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, one of the great jurists among the Companions of the Messenger, peace be upon him, that he said: 'What the Muslims consider good is good in the sight of Allah.' 4/6 - The Authority of Custom in Islamic Jurisprudence in Summary: Indeed, custom in Islamic jurisprudence has a legal consideration in building many types of rulings that we will see their detailed explanation in our discussion of the theory of custom in Islamic jurisprudence, at the end of the second part of this juristic introduction. And the juristic ijtihads in Islam are agreed upon this consideration for custom, although there is some variation among them in its limits and scope. And the jurists have given great weight to custom in the establishment and negation of rights among people in various aspects of transactions and types of actions, and they considered custom and habit an important principle and a great, vast source by which legal rulings among people are established according to it in everything that does not conflict with a specific legislative text that prevents it. So custom in their view is a sufficient legal proof in establishing binding rulings and detailed obligations among people wherever there is no other evidence; rather, it is left to it. (1) The mawquf athar is one in which the Companion speaks without attributing it to the Prophet. And it is established that if the mawquf athar contains a legislative matter, it is considered like a hadith narrated from the Prophet because the Companion cannot say it by his own opinion. And some jurists raise this text to the Prophet, but the verified view is that it is a mawquf athar from Ibn Mas'ud. Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated it in his Musnad, 1/379, as mawquf from him. (See: First Treatise on Publishing Custom in Building Some Rulings on Custom by Ibn 'Abidin).

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice accepted as a subsidiary source of law.
النص التشريعي الخاص (al-nass al-tashri'i al-khas): A specific legislative text from Quran or Sunnah that explicitly rules on a matter.
الالتزامات التفصيلية (al-iltizamat al-tafsiliyyah): Detailed obligations that arise from the application of custom.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Historical and Conceptual Order of Islamic Legal Sources
Historical and conceptual order of Islamic legal sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical order and conceptual hierarchy of the sources of Islamic fiqh according to the Hanafi school, and apply this hierarchy in prioritizing evidence when deriving a fatwa.

Core Idea
The sources of fiqh emerged historically in one order, but the conceptual hierarchy for a mufti is: Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, then Qiyas.

This passage from al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm traces how Islamic legal sources developed from the Prophet's era to the age of the Companions and later scholars. Understanding the difference between historical emergence and conceptual ranking is critical for a mufti: just because qiyas was used before ijma historically does not mean it takes precedence. The agreed-upon hierarchy places ijma above qiyas, and subsidiary sources like istihsan can override qiyas when they conflict.

Guided Path
1
Trace the historical emergence
Identify the sources as they appeared chronologically: initially only Qur'an and Sunnah, then the use of qiyas and istislah by the Companions, followed by the formal recognition of ijma in later centuries.
During the Prophet's lifetime, ijtihad was always subject to his approval; after his death, qiyas was used before ijma was codified.
2
Distinguish historical order from conceptual order
Note that qiyas predates ijma as a practiced method, but in the hierarchy of authority, ijma ranks above qiyas. The conceptual order is not the same as the historical sequence.
3
Memorize the conceptual hierarchy
The agreed order of priority when deriving rulings: (1) Qur'an, (2) Sunnah, (3) Ijma, (4) Qiyas. Subsidiary sources (istihsan, istislah, 'urf) come after but take precedence over qiyas when applicable.
This hierarchy is accepted by all four Sunni schools of law.
4
Understand the role of subsidiary sources
When qiyas conflicts with istihsan, istislah, or established custom ('urf), the subsidiary source overrides qiyas. This is a key feature of Hanafi usul.
Example: istihsan may permit a transaction that would be invalid under strict qiyas.
5
Apply the hierarchy in a fatwa scenario
Given a new case, check for explicit Qur'an and Sunnah texts. If none, search for a binding consensus (ijma). If no ijma, resort to qiyas, but consider whether any subsidiary source overrides it.
This sequential check ensures the fatwa aligns with the structure of Islamic legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: مصادر الفقه الإسلامي
:
لا يلبثون أن يعرضوا أفهامهم واجتهاداتهم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أول ما يعودون إليه، فيقرهم، أو يخطئهم ويصحح لهم.
أما بعد عصر الرسالة فقد كان أبو بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما يجمعان الصحابة في الحوادث ويشاورانهم. فإن لم يعرف أحد منهم فيها عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نصاً أو قضاء، واتفقوا فيها على حكم أنه هو مقتضى قواعد الشريعة عمل الخليفتان فيها بما اتفقوا عليه .
وإن لم يتفق الصحابة على حكم الحادثة اجتهد الخليفتان بآرائهما الحكم الأصلح والأقرب شبهاً بأقضية الرسول عملاً بدلالة القياس أو المصالح .
ثم بعد ذلك حدث في العصور التالية حاجة إلى تعرف ما اتفق عليه فقهاء الصحابة ليتخذ أساساً في فقه الشريعة.
ثم ألحق به في الاعتبار ما يتفق عليه الفقهاء التابعيون(١) من المجتهدين، لأنهم هم الراسخون في علم الشريعة ومرجع فهمها إليهم.
فأصبح الإجماع بعد عصر الرسالة مصدراً من مصادر الأحكام.
فإن لم يحصل هذا الإجماع، أو لم يثبت نقله وروايته في موضوع ما، لم يكن بد لكل فقيه أن يلجأ إلى الطريق التي كان يلجأ إليها الصحابة أنفسهم عندما يعوزهم النص، وذلك طريق الاجتهاد والرأي الذي سمي بعد قياساً أو استحساناً.
فيتلخص من ذلك أنه لم يكن في عصر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مصدر تشريعي سوى الكتاب والسنة ؛ وأن القياس كان موقتاً يعقبه استفسار وتصحيح؛ وأن الإجماع متأخر عن القياس وجوداً، وإن كان مقدّماً عليه رتبةً واعتباراً كما سنرى فيما يلي.
٢/٧ - ب - الترتيب الاعتباري :
ما تقدم هو الترتيب التاريخي في نشوء مصادر الفقه الإسلامي.
أما الترتيب الاعتباري بينها فقد اتفقت أئمة الإسلام وعلماء أصول الفقه أنَّ نصوص القرآن مقدَّمة في الاعتبار على كل ما سواها، ثم نصوص السنة، ثم الإجماع، ثم القياس.
وإذا تعارض حكم القياس مع حكم بعض المصادر التبعية من استحسان أو استصلاح أو عرف يقدم في الاعتبار حكم كل من المصادر الثلاثة على حكم القياس كما تقدم بيانه وأمثلته .
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Fiqh
:
They would not hesitate to present their understandings and ijtihads to the Prophet, peace be upon him, as soon as they returned to him, and he would either approve them or correct them.
After the era of the Message, Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, would gather the Companions in incidents and consult them. If none of them knew a text or judgment from the Prophet, and they agreed on a ruling that was in accordance with the principles of Sharia, the two caliphs would act upon what they agreed upon.
And if the Companions did not agree on a ruling for an incident, the two caliphs would exert ijtihad with their opinions for the most appropriate ruling that most resembled the judgments of the Messenger, acting by the indication of qiyas or istislah.
Then, after that, in later centuries, there arose a need to know what the Companion jurists had agreed upon to make it a foundation in fiqh.
Then what the Tabi'un jurists among the mujtahids agreed upon was appended to it in consideration, because they are the firmly grounded in the knowledge of Sharia and the reference for its understanding is to them.
Thus, after the era of the Message, ijma became a source of rulings.
If this ijma did not occur, or its transmission and narration in a matter was not established, every jurist had no choice but to resort to the method that the Companions themselves used when they lacked a text, which is the path of ijtihad and opinion, later called qiyas or istihsan.
It is concluded from this that during the time of the Prophet, there was no legislative source other than the Book and the Sunnah; that qiyas was temporary, followed by inquiry and correction; and that ijma is later than qiyas in existence, although it is prior to it in rank and consideration, as we will see below.
2/7 - B - The Conceptual Order:
What preceded is the historical order in the emergence of the sources of Islamic fiqh.
As for the conceptual order among them, the Imams of Islam and scholars of Usul al-Fiqh agreed that the texts of the Qur'an are given priority in consideration over everything else, then the texts of the Sunnah, then ijma, then qiyas.
And if a ruling of qiyas conflicts with a ruling of any of the subsidiary sources such as istihsan, istislah, or 'urf, the ruling of each of these three sources is given priority over the ruling of qiyas, as its explanation and examples have preceded.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' addresses the historical development and conceptual hierarchy of the sources of Islamic law (masadir al-fiqh). The author begins by describing the practice during the Prophet's lifetime: the only sources were the Qur'an and Sunnah, and any ijtihad by the Companions was subject to the Prophet's approval or correction. After the Prophet, the caliphs Abu Bakr and Umar consulted the Companions. If consensus (ijma) was reached on a ruling consistent with Sharia principles, it was adopted. If no consensus, they resorted to ijtihad via qiyas or istislah. Later, the consensus of the Companions and, subsequently, the Tabi'un (Followers) became a recognized source, forming ijma as an independent source. Importantly, ijma emerged after qiyas in practice, but comes before it in rank. The passage then clarifies the agreed-upon conceptual order: Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas. Subsidiary sources (istihsan, istislah, 'urf) take precedence over qiyas when they conflict. This lesson is fundamental for understanding how a Hanafi mufti prioritizes evidence: primary texts first, then consensus, then analogical reasoning, and finally subsidiary sources like istihsan (juristic preference) which in the Hanafi school often serves as a tool to override strict qiyas for public interest or custom.

Key Terms
مصادر الفقه الإسلامي (masadir al-fiqh al-islami): The sources from which Islamic legal rulings are derived, primarily the Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas, along with subsidiary sources.
الإجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahid scholars of a generation on a legal ruling.
القياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the agreed-upon conceptual order of the primary sources of Islamic fiqh?
A) Sunnah, Qur'an, Ijma, Qiyas
B) Qur'an, Sunnah, Qiyas, Ijma
C) Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas
D) Ijma, Qur'an, Sunnah, Qiyas

Key Takeaway
The conceptual hierarchy of sources—Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas—must govern a mufti's reasoning, overriding the historical sequence.

Historical vs Hierarchical Order of Secondary Sources of Fiqh
Parsing the distinction between historical sequence and legal rank of secondary sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the historical emergence and hierarchical precedence of Ijma', Qiyas, and subsidiary sources, and apply the hierarchical rule when sources conflict.

Core Idea
Ijma' is later historically but higher in rank; subsidiary sources override Qiyas.

This passage resolves a common confusion: the difference between when a source first appeared and its authority level. It clarifies that although Qiyas was used before Ijma' was formally established, Ijma' carries greater weight. Moreover, subsidiary sources like Istihsan can override Qiyas. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a mufti to correctly prioritize evidence when issuing fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish historical vs hierarchical order
Identify that the passage first mentions the historical order (Qiyas preceded Ijma' in usage) and then the hierarchical order (Ijma' ranks above Qiyas).
Do not conflate chronological appearance with legal authority.
2
State the agreed hierarchy
List the four primary sources in descending order: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas. This is the unanimous view.
This hierarchy is based on the strength of evidence (qat'i vs zanni).
3
Note the subsidiary sources exception
Recognise that Istihsan, Istislah, and custom (urf) take precedence over Qiyas when they conflict. This is a key Hanafi principle.
These subsidiary sources are not ranked above Quran/Sunnah/Ijma', only above Qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٧) الترتيب التاريخي والاعتباري لمصادر الفقه الإسلامي سوى الكتاب والسنة ؛ وأن القياس كان موقتاً يعقبه استفسار وتصحيح؛ وأن الإجماع متأخر عن القياس وجوداً، وإن كان مقدّماً عليه رتبةً واعتباراً كما سنرى فيما يلي. ٢/٧ - ب - الترتيب الاعتباري : ما تقدم هو الترتيب التاريخي في نشوء مصادر الفقه الإسلامي. أما الترتيب الاعتباري بينها فقد اتفقت أئمة الإسلام وعلماء أصول الفقه أنَّ نصوص القرآن مقدَّمة في الاعتبار على كل ما سواها، ثم نصوص السنة، ثم الإجماع، ثم القياس. وإذا تعارض حكم القياس مع حكم بعض المصادر التبعية من استحسان أو استصلاح أو عرف يقدم في الاعتبار حكم كل من المصادر الثلاثة على حكم القياس كما تقدم بيانه وأمثلته .
Chapter (7): The historical and hierarchical order of the sources of Islamic jurisprudence other than the Book and the Sunnah; and that Qiyas was temporary, followed by inquiry and correction; and that Ijma' is later than Qiyas in existence, although it is prior in rank and consideration as we will see below. 2/7 - B - The Hierarchical Order: What preceded is the historical order in the emergence of the sources of Islamic jurisprudence. As for the hierarchical order among them, the Imams of Islam and scholars of Usul al-Fiqh have agreed that the texts of the Quran are given precedence in consideration over everything else, then the texts of the Sunnah, then Ijma', then Qiyas. And if the ruling of Qiyas conflicts with the ruling of some subsidiary sources such as Istihsan, Istislah, or custom, then the ruling of each of the three sources is given precedence over the ruling of Qiyas, as has been previously explained with examples.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between the historical sequence and the hierarchical status of the sources of Islamic jurisprudence other than the Quran and Sunnah. It begins by stating that Qiyas was initially provisional, subject to verification, and that Ijma' historically came after Qiyas in existence, although in rank it is superior. This means that the chronological appearance of Qiyas preceded the formal recognition of Ijma', but Ijma' is of higher authority. Then it presents the hierarchical order agreed upon by the scholars: Quran, then Sunnah, then Ijma', then Qiyas. Finally, it notes that secondary sources like Istihsan (juristic preference), Istislah (public interest), and custom (urf) take precedence over Qiyas in case of conflict, as explained previously with examples. This reinforces the Hanafi principle that certain subsidiary sources can override analogy when they are based on stronger textual or rational considerations.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the ummah after the Prophet's death on a legal ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning from a known ruling (asl) to a new case (far') due to shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which source is given precedence in hierarchical order if Qiyas conflicts with Istihsan?
A) Qiyas
B) Istihsan
C) The Quran
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Ijma' ranks above Qiyas despite later historical appearance; subsidiary sources (istihsan, istislah, custom) override Qiyas in conflict.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Distinction Between Shariah and Fiqh
Defining Shariah and Fiqh and their relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Shariah from Fiqh and identify which category of fiqh rulings is open to ijtihad and scholarly disagreement.

Core Idea
Shariah is infallible revelation; Fiqh is fallible human understanding of that revelation.

Understanding the difference between Shariah (the divine texts) and Fiqh (the jurists' understanding) is fundamental for any mufti. This lesson from Mustafa al-Zarqa clarifies that while Shariah is sacred and infallible, Fiqh – even when based on texts – is a human endeavor open to error and discussion. Moreover, not all fiqh rulings are equal: some are fixed by definitive texts, while others are the proper domain of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of Shariah
Read the first part of the passage where al-Zarqa defines Shariah as the Qur'an and Sunnah. Note that he cites Quranic verses to prove the Prophet's speech and actions are by divine revelation.
The Sunnah includes both the Prophet's sayings and actions that explain and apply the Qur'an.
2
Distinguish Fiqh as human understanding
Now look at the definition of Fiqh: it is what scholars understand and deduce from Shariah texts. Note the key difference: Shariah is infallible, Fiqh is not.
Fiqh includes the rules and principles derived from texts, but it is the work of jurists and therefore open to correction.
3
Parse the two types of fiqh rulings
Identify the two categories al-Zarqa presents: (1) rulings from definitive (qat'i) texts, and (2) rulings from non-definitive texts or matters left silent. Understand that only the second type is subject to ijtihad and scholarly difference.
The first type, like the obligation of prayer and zakat, is fixed and not open to reinterpretation.
4
Apply the distinction to a mufti's work
For a mufti, this distinction means that when issuing a fatwa, you must identify whether the ruling falls under the qat'i category (which is binding and not open to disagreement) or the zanni category (where your own ijtihad or reliance on a school opinion is legitimate).
This also affects how you present the fatwa: qat'i rulings are stated firmly; zanni rulings may be presented as one of several valid opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن
الفرق بين الشريعة والفقه
١/٨ - من المهم جداً التمييز بين قولنا: الشريعة الإسلامية، وقولنا: الفقه الإسلامي. فالشريعة هي نصوص القرآن الموحى به من الله تعالى إلى رسوله محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم والسنة النبوية، وهي أقوال الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وأفعاله التي هي شرح وتفصيل لما أجمله القرآن وتطبيق عملي لأوامره ونواهيه وإباحاته، باعتبار أن كل ما يصدر عن الرسول الله متصلاً بتفسير الشريعة وتطبيقها ليس من عند نفسه ومن رأيه الشخصي، وإنما هو بوحي من الله تعالى إليه، وفقاً لقوله تعالى في القرآن: ﴿مَا ضَلَّ صَاحِبُكُمْ وَمَا غَوَىٰ * وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ الْهَوَىٰ﴾ (سورة النجم الآيتين ٣ و ٤) وقوله: ﴿وَمَا آتَاكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ وَمَا نَهَاكُمْ عَنْهُ فَانتَهُوا﴾ الآية (سورة الحشر ٥٩/٧)
٢/٨ - أما الفقه فهو ما يفهمه العلماء من نصوص الشريعة وما يستنبطونه من تلك النصوص، ويقررونه ويؤصلونه وما يقعدونه من القواعد المستمدة من دلالات النصوص. ولا يجوز الخلط وعدم التمييز بين مفهوم الشريعة الإسلامية، ومفهوم الفقه الإسلامي، لأن الشريعة معصومة وهي في العقيدة الإسلامية صواب وخير كلها تهدي الحياة الإنسانية إلى الطريق السليم المستقيم. أما الفقه فهو من عمل الفقهاء في طريق فهم الشريعة وتطبيق نصوصها وفقاً لغرض الشارع والقواعد الأصولية في استنباط الأحكام منها. وفي هذا يختلف فهم فقيه عن فهم فقيه آخر وفهم كل واحد مهما علا قدره يحتمل الخطأ والصواب لأنه غير معصوم وليس معنى ذلك أنه لا قيمة له، بل له قيمة عظيمة وتقدير كبير، ولكن المقصود أن ليس له القدسية التي للشريعة نفسها المتمثلة بنصوصها من الكتاب والسنة الثابتة فالفقه وهو فهم الفقيه ورأيه ولو كان مبنياً على النص الشرعي، هو قابل للمناقشة والتصويب والتخطئة، ولكن التخطئة تنصرف إلى فهم الفقيه لا إلى تخطئة النص الشرعي. ومن ثم اختلفت آراء الفقهاء ورد بعضهم على بعض، وخطأ بعضهم بعضاً.
٣/٨ - على أن هناك نقطة مهمة محل اشتباه ينبغي تجليتها والتنبيه عليها، وهي أن الفقه الإسلامي يتضمن نوعين من الأحكام مختلفين في طبيعتهما:
- النوع الأول: أحكام قررتها نصوص قطعية الثبوت والدلالة تمثل إرادة الشارع الإسلامي الواضحة فيما يفرضه على المكلفين نظاماً للإسلام ملزماً لهم، لم يترك لتفسيرهم وفهمهم واستنتاجاتهم. وذلك مثل أصل الصلاة والزكاة وصوم رمضان والوفاء بالعقود، والجهاد بحسب الحاجة وقدر الطاقة، ونحو ذلك مما جاءت به النصوص في الكتاب والسنة المتواترة.
- النوع الثاني: أحكام سكت عنها الكتاب والسنة وتركت للاجتهاد واستنتاج علماء الشريعة أو جاءت بها نصوص غير قطعية الثبوت أو الدلالة تحتمل اختلاف آراء العلماء في ثبوتها أو في دلالتها، وهي محل اجتهادهم في فهمها واستنتاج الأحكام منها.
فالفقه الإسلامي ومدوناته تتضمن كلا النوعين هذين. فما قلناه عن الفرق بين الشريعة والفقه منصرف إلى هذا النوع الثاني من الأحكام الفقهية الذي هو استنتاج الفقهاء واجتهادهم في تفسير النصوص المحتملة غير القطعية.
Chapter Eight: The Difference Between Shariah and Fiqh
1/8 - It is very important to distinguish between our saying: Islamic Shariah, and our saying: Islamic Fiqh. Shariah is the texts of the Qur'an revealed by Allah Almighty to His Messenger Muhammad (peace be upon him) and the Prophetic Sunnah, which are the sayings of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and his actions that are an explanation and detail of what the Qur'an summarized and a practical application of its commands, prohibitions, and permissions, considering that everything that comes from the Messenger of Allah related to the interpretation and application of Shariah is not from himself or his personal opinion, but is by revelation from Allah Almighty to him, according to His saying in the Qur'an: 'Your companion has not strayed, nor has he erred, nor does he speak from [his own] desire' (Surah An-Najm, verses 3-4) and His saying: 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it, and whatever he forbids you, abstain from it' (Surah Al-Hashr, 59/7).
2/8 - As for Fiqh, it is what the scholars understand from the texts of Shariah and what they deduce from those texts, and what they determine, establish, and formulate from the derived rules from the indications of the texts. And it is not permissible to confuse and fail to distinguish between the concept of Islamic Shariah and the concept of Islamic Fiqh, because Shariah is infallible and in Islamic creed is all correctness and goodness, guiding human life to the straight path. As for Fiqh, it is the work of jurists in the way of understanding Shariah and applying its texts according to the purpose of the Lawgiver and the principles of usul in deriving rulings from them. In this, one jurist's understanding differs from another jurist's understanding, and the understanding of each one, however high his rank, is susceptible to error and correctness because he is not infallible. This does not mean it has no value; rather, it has great value and high esteem, but the point is that it does not have the sacredness that Shariah itself has, represented by its texts from the Book and the confirmed Sunnah. So Fiqh, which is the jurist's understanding and opinion, even if based on a legal text, is open to discussion, correction, and criticism, but criticism is directed to the jurist's understanding, not to criticizing the legal text. Hence, the opinions of jurists differed, and some refuted others, and some declared others wrong.
3/8 - However, there is an important point subject to confusion that should be clarified and pointed out, namely that Islamic Fiqh includes two types of rulings different in their nature:
- The first type: rulings established by texts definitive in authenticity and indication, representing the clear will of the Islamic Lawgiver regarding what He imposes on the legally responsible individuals as a binding system of Islam, not left to their interpretation, understanding, and deductions. Such as the obligation of prayer, zakat, fasting Ramadan, fulfilling contracts, and jihad according to need and capability, and the like of what has come in the texts of the Qur'an and the mutawatir Sunnah.
- The second type: rulings about which the Book and Sunnah are silent and left to the ijtihad and deduction of Shariah scholars, or which have come through texts not definitive in authenticity or indication, allowing for differences of opinion among scholars regarding their authenticity or indication, and they are subject to their ijtihad in understanding them and deriving rulings from them.
So Islamic Fiqh and its compilations include both of these two types. What we have said about the difference between Shariah and Fiqh is directed to this second type of fiqh rulings, which is the deduction of jurists and their ijtihad in interpreting the probable, non-definitive texts.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a foundational text for understanding the relationship between divine revelation (Shariah) and human legal reasoning (Fiqh). The author begins by defining Shariah as the Qur'an and Sunnah, emphasizing that everything the Prophet did or said in relation to the law was by divine revelation, citing Quranic proofs. He then defines Fiqh as the human understanding and deduction of scholars from these sources, stressing that Fiqh is fallible and open to discussion, unlike the infallible Shariah. Finally, al-Zarqa distinguishes two categories of rulings within Fiqh: (1) those based on definitive (qat'i) texts which are fixed and not subject to ijtihad, such as the obligations of prayer and zakat; and (2) those based on probable (zanni) texts or matters left to ijtihad, which are the proper domain of fiqh and scholarly disagreement. This distinction is crucial for a mufti: it prevents conflating the sacredness of the primary sources with the derived rulings, and it clarifies that the authority of a fatwa depends on the type of ruling. For Hanafis, this aligns with their usul where rulings from mutawatir texts are qat'i, while khabar wahid leads to zann. The author's point that Fiqh is not sacred but still valuable underscores the respect for scholarly work while maintaining the primacy of revelation.

Key Terms
الشريعة (al-Shari'ah): The divine texts of the Qur'an and the Prophetic Sunnah as revelation, infallible and binding.
الفقه (al-Fiqh): The understanding and deduction of scholars from the Shariah texts, which is human ijtihad, fallible, and open to discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, which type of rulings in Islamic Fiqh is subject to scholarly disagreement and ijtihad?
A) Rulings established by definitive (qat'i) texts
B) Rulings from the Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah only
C) Rulings from non-definitive texts or matters left to ijtihad
D) All rulings of Fiqh are equally subject to ijtihad

Key Takeaway
Shariah is the infallible divine text; Fiqh is the fallible human interpretation, and only the second type of fiqh rulings (based on zanni evidence) is subject to ijtihad and disagreement.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Two Types of Fiqh Rulings: Definitive Texts and Ijtihad
Distinguishing two categories of fiqh rulings based on certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rulings derived from qat'i texts and those from ijtihad, and explain how this distinction affects the authority and revisability of fiqh rulings.

Core Idea
Rulings from definitive texts are sacred and immutable; ijtihadi rulings are human products, open to discussion and correction.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam clarifies a foundational distinction that every mufti must internalize. Not all fiqh rulings carry the same weight—some are directly from clear, definitive texts and are beyond ijtihad, while others are the result of juristic reasoning and are subject to refinement. This distinction shapes the methodology of ifta' and the humility required of the jurist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Categories
Read the passage carefully and note the two types of rulings described. The first type comes from texts that are qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalala (definitive transmission and meaning). The second type comes from non-qat'i texts, ijtihad, qiyas, istihsan, or masalih mursalah.
2
Analyze the Basis of Each Type
For the first type, the ruling is directly from the Lawgiver and is not open to interpretation. For the second, the ruling is a human deduction from probabilistic evidence. Reflect on how this affects the ruling's authority.
3
Understand the Implications for Ifta'
Consider how a mufti approaches each type. For the first, he must apply it without deviation. For the second, he may choose among valid opinions, weigh evidences, and consider contemporary context. This distinction prevents both rigid literalism and unchecked relativism.
4
Apply to a Mufti's Practice
Given a scenario, determine whether the ruling falls into the first or second type. Practice distinguishing between a text with clear imperative (e.g., 'Establish prayer') and a text that is ambiguous or a matter of ijtihad (e.g., the exact method of calculating zakat on crops).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: مصادر الفقه الإسلامي وفقاً لغرض الشارع والقواعد الأصولية في استنباط الأحكام منها. وفي هذا يختلف فهم فقيه عن فهم فقيه آخر وفهم كل واحد مهما علا قدره يحتمل الخطأ والصواب لأنه غير معصوم وليس معنى ذلك أنه لا قيمة له، بل له قيمة عظيمة وتقدير كبير، ولكن المقصود أن ليس له القدسية التي للشريعة نفسها المتمثلة بنصوصها من الكتاب والسنة الثابتة فالفقه وهو فهم الفقيه ورأيه ولو كان مبنياً على النص الشرعي، هو قابل للمناقشة والتصويب والتخطئة، ولكن التخطئة تنصرف إلى فهم الفقيه لا إلى تخطئة النص الشرعي. ومن ثم اختلفت آراء الفقهاء ورد بعضهم على بعض، وخطأ بعضهم بعضاً ..
۳/۸ - على أن هناك نقطة مهمة محل اشتباه ينبغي تجليتها والتنبيه أن الفقه الإسلامي يتضمن نوعين من الأحكام مختلفين في طبيعتهما :
- النوع الأول: أحكام قررتها نصوص قطعية الثبوت والدلالة تمثل إرادة الشارع الإسلامي الواضحة فيما يفرضه على المكلفين نظاماً للإسلام ملزماً لهم، لم يترك لتفسيرهم وفهمهم واستنتاجاتهم. وذلك مثل أصل الصلاة والزكاة وصوم رمضان والوفاء بالعقود، والجهاد بحسب الحاجة وقدر الطاقة، ونحو ذلك مما جاءت به النصوص في الكتاب والسنة المتواترة .
- النوع الثاني: أحكام سكت عنها الكتاب والسنة وتركت للاجتهاد واستنتاج علماء الشريعة أو جاءت بها نصوص غير قطعية الثبوت أو الدلالة تحتمل اختلاف آراء العلماء في ثبوتها أو في دلالتها، وهي محل اجتهادهم في فهمها واستنتاج الأحكام منها .
فالفقه الإسلامي ومدوناته تتضمن كلا النوعين هذين. فما قلناه عن الفرق بين الشريعة والفقه منصرف إلى هذا النوع الثاني من الأحكام الفقهية الذي هو استنتاج الفقهاء واجتهادهم في تفسير النصوص المحتملة غير القطعية الدلالة أو من أقيستهم، أو ما قرروه بطريق الاستحسان حيث يرون سبباً يقتضي الخروج عن حكم القياس، أو ما قرروه بطريق الاستصلاح والمصالح المرسلة حيث لا نص يحكم في الموضوع، وإنما قرروا فيه الحكم نتيجة للموازنة بين ما فيه من مصلحة أو ضرر، ونحو ذلك من الأحكام الاجتهادية، وهي أكثر ما يتضمنه فقه المذاهب .
فهذا النوع هو الذي من عمل الفقهاء واستنتاجهم، ولا يتمتع بالقدسية التي للنصوص التشريعية وهو الذي سندرس أدوار تطوره في الباب الثالث التالي، أما النوع الأول فله قدسية النصوص التشريعية نفسها، وثباتها.
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Fiqh according to the purpose of the Lawgiver and the usuli rules for deriving rulings from them. In this, the understanding of one faqih differs from the understanding of another faqih, and the understanding of each one, no matter how high his rank, is susceptible to error and correctness because he is not infallible. This does not mean that it has no value; rather it has great value and high esteem, but the intended meaning is that it does not have the sanctity that belongs to the Sharia itself, manifested in its texts from the Book and the established Sunnah. Fiqh, which is the understanding of the faqih and his opinion, even if based on the legal text, is open to discussion, correction, and criticism. But criticism is directed at the understanding of the faqih, not at criticizing the legal text. Hence the opinions of jurists differed, some refuted others, and some declared others mistaken.
3/8 - However, there is an important point of confusion that should be clarified, and that is that Islamic fiqh includes two types of rulings that differ in their nature:
- The first type: Rulings established by texts that are qat'i in thubut (definitive transmission) and dalala (definitive meaning), representing the clear will of the Islamic Lawgiver regarding what He imposes on the obligated as a binding system of Islam, not left to their interpretation, understanding, and deductions. Such as the obligation of prayer, zakat, fasting Ramadan, fulfilling contracts, jihad according to need and capacity, and the like, which are established by texts in the Book and the mutawatir Sunnah.
- The second type: Rulings about which the Book and Sunnah are silent and left to ijtihad and deduction of Sharia scholars, or which come from texts that are not qat'i in thubut or dalala, thus admitting differences of scholars' opinions regarding their authenticity or meaning, and they are subject to their ijtihad in understanding them and deriving rulings from them.
So Islamic fiqh and its compilations contain both of these types. What we said about the difference between Sharia and Fiqh applies to this second type of fiqh rulings, which is the deduction of jurists and their ijtihad in interpreting texts that are not qat'i in meaning, or from their analogies, or what they established by way of istihsan where they see a reason requiring departure from the ruling of qiyas, or what they established by way of istislah and masalih mursalah where there is no text governing the subject, but they establish the ruling as a result of balancing between what is of benefit or harm in it, and the like of these ijtihadi rulings. And these constitute most of what the fiqh of the schools contains.
So this type is the work of the jurists and their deduction, and it does not enjoy the sanctity of the legislative texts. And it is this type whose stages of development we will study in the following third chapter. As for the first type, it has the sanctity of the legislative texts themselves and their permanence.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal Al-Fiqhi Al-Aam by Mustafa Ahmad Al-Zarqa introduces a foundational distinction for understanding Islamic law. The author opens by situating this discussion within the broader context of the sources of fiqh according to the purpose of the Lawgiver (Shari') and the usuli rules of istinbat. He then makes a critical epistemological point: the understanding (fiqh) of any jurist, regardless of his stature, is fallible and open to criticism because no one is infallible (ma'sum). This does not diminish its value; rather, it means it lacks the sanctity (qudsiyya) that belongs to the Sharia itself as embodied in the definitive texts of the Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah. Importantly, criticism is directed at the jurist's understanding, not at the text.

The author then clarifies a key point of confusion: Islamic fiqh contains two fundamentally different types of rulings. The first type consists of rulings established by texts that are qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalala — definitive in both transmission and meaning. These represent the clear will of the Lawgiver and are not open to juristic interpretation. Examples include the obligation of prayer, zakat, fasting Ramadan, fulfilling contracts, and jihad within capacity. These rulings have the sanctity of the texts themselves and are fixed.

The second type comprises rulings about which the texts are silent or which come from texts that are not qat'i in transmission or meaning. These are subject to ijtihad and deduction by scholars. They include rulings derived from interpretation of ambiguous texts, qiyas (analogy), istihsan (juristic preference), and istislah/masalih mursalah (public interest). The author emphasizes that this second type constitutes the majority of the fiqh found in the schools. It is the product of human reasoning and lacks the sanctity of the definitive texts. The study of the historical development of this type is promised for a later chapter.

This passage is crucial for understanding the relationship between Sharia (divine law) and Fiqh (human understanding). It warns against conflating the two and clarifies that while Fiqh is authoritative within its scope, it is not infallible. This directly impacts the methodology of ifta': a mufti must recognize which type of ruling he is dealing with and accord it its proper weight.

Key Terms
الشريعة (al-sharī‘ah): The divine law as revealed in the Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah, which has sanctity and is infallible.
الفقه (al-fiqh): The understanding and opinion of the jurist, derived from Sharia sources, open to discussion and correction.
نصوص قطعية الثبوت والدلالة (nuṣūṣ qaṭ‘iyyat al-thubūt wa al-dalālah): Texts with definitive transmission (e.g., mutawatir) and clear, unambiguous meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of rulings is the product of jurists' ijtihad and lacks the sanctity of the Sharia texts?
A) Rulings from texts that are qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalala
B) Rulings from texts that are not qat'i in transmission or meaning, or from ijtihad
C) Rulings from the mutawatir Sunnah
D) All rulings in Islamic fiqh

Key Takeaway
The distinction between qat'i and ijtihadi rulings is foundational for understanding the scope of ijtihad and the authority of fiqh: muftis must recognize which type they are dealing with.

The Difference Between Sharia and Fiqh: Authority of Ijtihadi Rulings
Defining the distinction between primary revelation and ijtihadi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rulings derived directly from primary texts (sharia) and rulings derived through ijtihad (fiqh), and evaluate their respective authority in fatwa.

Core Idea
Ijtihadi rulings are human constructs; they lack the sanctity of the primary texts.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa draws a sharp line between the Sharia (the direct rulings of the Qur'an and Sunnah) and the Fiqh produced by jurists through methods like qiyas, istihsan, and istislah. This distinction is fundamental for a mufti: knowing which rulings are divine and immutable, and which are interpretive and subject to revision, shapes the entire practice of ifta.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of rulings
The author contrasts the first type (primary texts – Qur'an and Sunnah) with the second type (ijtihadi rulings derived by jurists). Note that the second type is the focus of the passage.
2
List the ijtihadi methods mentioned
The passage explicitly mentions qiyas (analogy), istihsan (juristic preference), istislah (public interest), and balancing benefit and harm (maslahah and darar).
These are tools of ijtihad in the Hanafi school and beyond.
3
State the authority difference
The ijtihadi rulings do not possess the sanctity (qudsiyyah) of legislative texts. They are the work of jurists and are subject to change.
This is crucial for fatwa: Ijtihadi rulings are not infallible.
4
Connect to the mufti's role
A mufti must be aware of this distinction when issuing a fatwa. Rulings from primary texts are binding and cannot be overridden; ijtihadi rulings may be weighed against other considerations and can evolve.
This echoes the Hanafi principle that ijtihad does not abrogate nass.

Source Text

This passage is a legal philosophy discussion distinguishing between two types of rulings: those directly from the primary texts (Sharia) and those derived by juristic reasoning (Fiqh). Key Terms
الشريعة (ash-sharīʿah): The primary divine texts (Qur'an and Sunnah) with their direct rulings, possessing sanctity and permanence.
الفقه (al-fiqh): Rulings derived by jurists through ijtihad, qiyas, istihsan, etc., lacking the sanctity of primary texts.

Foundational Textual Sources: Quran and Hadith in Hanafi Legal Theory
Survey of foundational Quranic and prophetic texts as primary legal sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths as primary textual evidence (dalil naqli) in Hanafi legal theory and explain how they serve as raw material for deriving legal rulings.

Core Idea
Quranic verses and authentic hadith are the primary raw material from which Hanafi jurists derive legal rulings.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-’Amm' surveys key Quranic verses and Prophetic traditions that form the foundational textual sources for Islamic law. These are not presented as ready-made rulings but as the primary evidence (adilla) from which Hanafi jurists, using principles of usul, derive specific legal rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source type
Determine whether each cited text is a Quranic verse (ayah) or a prophetic hadith. Both are examples of dalil naqli (textual evidence).
The Quranic verses are clearly marked with surah and ayah numbers; the hadiths are introduced with 'min al-hadith al-nabawi'.
2
Classify by legal theme
Group the verses by subject matter: contracts (5:1), prohibition of unjust consumption (2:188, 4:29), trusts and justice (4:58), orphan's property (17:34), divorce (2:229), foster care (2:233), retaliation (42:40), testimony (65:2, 2:283), debt (2:280), ease and necessity (2:185, 22:78, 2:173). Note the diversity of topics covered.
The hadiths cover general principles: harm, intention, liability, pardon, and guaranty.
3
Recognize the function as raw material
Understand that the author is not deriving rulings here; he is listing the raw material. Each verse or hadith is a source text that jurists will later analyze using usul categories (amm, khass, mutlaq, etc.) to extract legal rulings.
For example, the verse 5:1 is a general command (amm) that Hanafis interpret as obligating fulfillment of contracts.
4
Connect to Hanafi usul
Recall that in Hanafi usul, the Quran is qat'i al-thubut (definitive transmission), while hadith may be zanni al-thubut (probable). The school applies specific principles to derive rulings: e.g., al-amm yuqarrar 'ala umumihi hatta yarida dalil al-takhsis.
The hadiths, though fewer in number, often become the basis of legal maxims (qawaid kulliya).
5
Note the role of hadith as principle-sources
Several hadith express broad legal maxims: 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm) becomes the maxim 'al-darar yuzal'; 'innama al-a'malu bi-niyat' underlies all intentional acts. These are not just rulings but foundational principles for deducing further rules.
The Hanafi school often uses these hadith as overarching principles in istihsan and qiyas.

The Quranic Method of Universal Principles (القواعد الكلية)
Explanation of Quranic legislative methodology: general principles, not detailed branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Quran's general principles make it suitable for all times and apply this understanding to justify the Hanafi reliance on qawa'id kulliyah in fatwa.

Core Idea
The Quran sets foundational principles, not detailed rules, making it universally applicable and requiring muftis to derive rulings through ijtihad.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa explains a cornerstone of Islamic legal philosophy: the Quran deliberately abstains from enumerating every specific ruling. Instead, it lays down comprehensive universal principles (qawa'id kulliyah) rooted in human fitrah. For a Hanafi mufti, this methodology is essential — it means that fiqh rulings are not static proof texts but living derivations from general axioms. Understanding this prevents the common error of demanding Quranic verses for every detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic Method
Find the phrases that describe how the Quran presents rulings: it lays foundations (يضع الأسس), erects universal principles (ينصب القواعد الكلية), and summarizes commands (يجمل الأوامر).
Compare to the Hanafi emphasis on generality (umum) and principles.
2
Understand the Rationale for Timelessness
The author argues these principles are above theoretical differences because they connect to human instinct (الفطرة) and perception (الإدراك الإنساني). This makes the Quran suitable for every time and place.
This is a key argument against those who claim the Quran is outdated.
3
Identify the 'Constitutional' Nature
The ayat al-ahkam are described as دستورية شاملة جامعة — comprehensive constitutional verses to which all details revert. This means they serve as axioms from which rules are derived, not as exhaustive codes.
For a mufti, this justifies extensive use of qiyas and istihsan.
4
Apply to Hanafi Iftaa
When issuing a fatwa, a Hanafi mufti should first identify the relevant universal principle from the Quran, then derive the detailed ruling using usul tools (Sunnah, qiyas, istihsan). Never expect the Quran to explicitly mention every case.
This aligns with the Hanafi view that the Quran is the primary source but not the only one.
5
Examine the Hadith Example
The author cites the hadith 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر' as an example: it is hasan, not in Bukhari/Muslim, but accepted because it aligns with Quranic justice principles and was practiced by the Prophet and Companions.
This shows that even weaker narrations can be used when they fit the Quran's universal principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا سابقاً من أسلوب القرآن الكريم في بيان شرائع الأحكام أنه يضع الأسس، وينصب القواعد الكلية، ويجمل الأوامر ولا يتناول فروع الأحكام وتفصيل جزئياتها إلا قليلاً في بعض المسائل.
فهذه الكليات الأساسية والقواعد الصحيحة التي يقوم عليها بنيان العدل في الحياة الاجتماعية هي في الغالب دائماً فوق متناول الاختلافات النظرية الفرعية في جميع البيئات، لأنها تتصل بالفطرة والإدراك الإنساني، فجاء القرآن كاشفاً عن قضاياها الثابتة فكان صالحاً لكل زمان ومكان. ولأنه لم يخض في التفصيلات كانت آيات الأحكام على قلتها دستورية شاملة جامعة ترجع إليها كل أحكام الشريعة وتفاصيلها.
=
ولذلك كان القرآن الكريم عند علماء الشريعة الإسلامية هو الحجة
ولكن يشهد له الواقع العملي في أقضية الرسول والصحابة. وقال في «الفتح» ۲۸۲/۵ في كتاب الشهادات وهذه الزيادة (أي: البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر ) ليست في الصحيحين، وإسنادها حسن.
And we have previously shown from the style of the Noble Quran in explaining the laws of rulings that it lays foundations, erects universal principles, and summarizes commands, and does not address branches of rulings and details of their particulars except rarely in some issues. So these fundamental universals and correct principles upon which the edifice of justice in social life is based are generally always above theoretical subsidiary differences in all environments, because they are connected to human instinct and perception. So the Quran came revealing its constant issues, and thus it was suitable for every time and place. And because it did not delve into details, its verses of rulings, though few, are comprehensive constitutional ones to which all rulings of Shariah and their details return. = Therefore, the Noble Quran in the view of Islamic Shariah scholars is the proof. But practical reality in the rulings of the Messenger and the Companions testifies to it. And he said in al-Fath (5/282) in the book of testimonies: 'And this addition (i.e., 'the burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the one who denies') is not in the Two Sahihs, and its chain is hasan.'
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage articulates a key principle of Islamic legal philosophy: the Quran's method is to lay down broad, universal principles (أسس وقواعد كلية) rather than detailed subsidiary rules. The author argues that this approach is intentional, allowing the Quran to be suitable for all times and places (صالحة لكل زمان ومكان). Because these principles are rooted in human fitrah (innate disposition), they transcend theoretical disagreements in different environments. The few ayat al-ahkam (verses of rulings) are not numerous, but they are constitutional (دستورية) — meaning they serve as foundational sources from which all detailed rulings are derived. This methodology echoes the Hanafi view that the Quran is the primary source (الحجة) but requires the Sunnah and ijtihad to apply its principles to specific cases. The author then notes a supporting point: the well-known hadith 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر' is considered hasan in chain according to al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in Fath al-Bari, though it is not in Bukhari and Muslim. This demonstrates that even hadith not of the highest authenticity can be used when they align with Quranic principles and practical application by the Prophet and Companions. For the Hanafi mufti, this passage reinforces the importance of working from general Quranic principles and not expecting every detail to be spelled out in the Quran itself.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa‘id al-kulliyah): Universal principles that are general in scope and serve as foundations for deriving detailed rulings.
دستورية (dusturiyyah): Constitutional: meaning foundational and comprehensive, to which all detailed rulings refer back.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it includes detailed rulings for every possible case.
B) Because it establishes universal foundations connected to human instinct and perception.
C) Because it was revealed in Arabic and can be interpreted flexibly.
D) Because it abrogates all previous scriptures.

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes universal principles, not detailed rules, making it timeless and requiring active ijtihad.

The Quran as a Foundational Source and Its Universal Principles
Explaining the Quran's constitutional role and its adaptability through universal principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Quran and Sunnah provide universal principles rather than detailed rulings, and apply the method of deriving general legal maxims from specific prophetic texts.

Core Idea
The Quran and Sunnah lay foundations; their genius is adaptability through ijtihad.

This lesson explores the nature of Quranic legislation as described by Mustafa al-Zarqa. You will see how a single hadith on preemption can form the basis for the modern legal concept of prescription. Understanding this will help you appreciate the Quran's role as a constitutional source that remains relevant across time and place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic legislative style
Read the passage where the author states that the Quran lays foundations, erects universal rules, and summarizes commands rather than detailing branches. This is the key to its timelessness.
The Quran's style is to establish principles, not exhaustive codes.
2
Examine the hadith example
Focus on the hadith: 'الشفعة كحل العقال' (Preemption is like loosening a hobble). The author uses this as an example of how a concise prophetic statement can serve as a basis for a general legal concept.
This hadith implies urgency and promptness in exercising the right of shuf'a.
3
Understand the derivation of prescription
See how the author connects the hadith to the idea of taqadum (prescription/limitation of time). The simile of loosening a hobble suggests that if one delays, the right is lost; this can be extended to other rights requiring timely action.
This is an example of expanding a specific text (takhrij al-furu' 'ala al-usul).
4
Consider the Quran's universality
Note the author's argument: because the Quran does not delve into details, its rulings are suitable for every time and place. The universal principles (al-ahkam al-kulliyya) transcend temporal and local differences.
This is why the Quran's legal content is small but foundational.
5
Appreciate the Quran as the primary source
The passage ends by noting that some jurists consider the Quran the sole source, with other sources being explications. This underscores its supreme authority in the hierarchy of Islamic law.
Hanafis prioritize the Quran but also accept Sunnah, ijma', and qiyas as sources.

Thus the Quran came revealing its fixed issues, so it was suitable for every time and place. Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam discusses the first era of Islamic jurisprudence (the Prophetic era) and the nature of Quranic legislation. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, emphasizes that the Quran and prophetic hadiths serve as foundational texts ('dasaatir') that provide universal principles rather than detailed branch rulings. He gives the example of the hadith 'al-shuf'a ka-hall al-'iqal' (preemption is like loosening the hobble) to illustrate how a concise prophetic statement can form the basis for the modern legal concept of prescription (taqadum), i.e., the limitation of time for claiming rights. This shows the Quran's and Sunnah's adaptability. The author then reiterates that the Quran's style is to establish broad rules and general commands, leaving detailed elaboration to later ijtihad. This makes the Quran suitable for all times and places, as it does not get entangled in transient particulars. The passage also notes that some jurists consider the Quran the sole source of Sharia, with all other sources merely explicating or deriving from it. This underscores the Quran's constitutional role as the foundational reference for all Islamic legal rulings.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): A preemptive right of a co-owner to purchase the share of another co-owner when sold to a third party.
التقادم (al-taqadum): The passage of time that results in extinguishing or limiting the right to judicial claim; statute of limitations.
الأحكام الكلية (al-ahkam al-kulliyya): Universal or general rulings that serve as foundational principles in Islamic law.
المصدر الوحيد (al-masdar al-wahid): The sole source; some jurists consider the Quran the only source of law, with others being mere explications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the significance of the hadith 'الشفعة كحل العقال' for contemporary legal systems?
A) It directly determines the rules of preemption in all cases.
B) It serves as a basis for the concept of limitation of actions (statute of limitations).
C) It is irrelevant to modern law and should not be applied.
D) It overrides all other sources of law in contractual matters.

Key Takeaway
The Quran and Sunnah provide universal principles that allow Islamic law to adapt to all times and places through ijtihad.

Suspension of Mu'allafatu Qulubuhum Payments
First example of Umar's ijtihad with detailed analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that Umar's suspension of payments to mu'allafatu qulubuhum was a temporary ijtihad, not abrogation, and apply the principle that the ruling remains available for future need.

Core Idea
Umar did not abrogate the zakah share for reconciliation; he suspended it for a specific group when the need ceased.

This lesson examines Caliph Umar's decision to stop giving zakah funds to a category of recipients known as 'those whose hearts are to be won over' (mu'allafatu qulubuhum). Some contemporaries thought this was an abrogation of the Qur'anic verse that prescribes this category. However, as the passage explains, Umar's ijtihad was a temporary suspension based on changed circumstances, not a nullification of the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and its original purpose
The mu'allafatu qulubuhum were given zakah or state funds to attract them to Islam or prevent their harm when Islam was weak.
2
Understand Umar's reasoning
Umar saw that after Islam became strong, the need to purchase their allegiance was gone. The category itself remains valid in principle.
3
Distinguish suspension from abrogation
The author stresses that Umar did not abrogate the text; he only withheld the gift from specific individuals because the wisdom behind the ruling (tending to needs of Islam) was no longer present for them.
4
Apply the principle to future scenarios
If a similar need arises (e.g., political propaganda), the state may allocate funds from this share again. The ruling is not cancelled; it is dormant until the need returns.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
