الفصل (۱۱) الدور الثاني : عصر الخلفاء الراشدين

۲ - وكذا اجتهاد عمر في وقف تنفيذ حد السرقة على السارقين في عام المجاعة المسمى: (عام) الرّمادة» ؛ واكتفائه بتعزير السارق بدلاً من قطع يده. وهذا من عمر ليس تعطيلاً لحد السرقة الشرعي، بل هو اجتهاد حكيم منه في تطبيق شرائط هذه العقوبة، لأنَّ من شرائطها شرعاً أن لا يكون السارق مضطراً إلى السرقة اضطراراً. فقد اعتبر عمر في ذلك شبهة عامة في أنهم كانوا يسرقون عن ضرورة ملجئة وقد قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات (۱) .

٣ - وكذلك إطلاقه النكاح لزوجة المفقود بعد أربع سنين من فقده دون انتظار تحقق وفاته أو موت لداته - أي أقرانه في العمر ـ كما سلف ذكره في بحث المصالح المرسلة. (ر: ف ١٢/٥).

(۱) روي من حديث عائشة، ومن حديث علي ومن حديث أبي هريرة، أما حديث عائشة فأخرجه الترمذي (١٤٢٤) في الحدود، بلفظ: ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم فإن كان له مخرج فخلوا سبيله، فإن الإمام أن يخطىء في العفو خير من يخطىء في العقوبة وضعف رفعه وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك ٣٨٤/٤، وقال: صحيح الإسناد، وتعقبه الذهبي. وأما حديث علي فأخرجه الدارقطني ٨٤/٣ وأما حديث أبي هريرة فأخرجه ابن ماجه (٢٥٤٥) بلفظ : ادفعوا الحدود ما وجدتم له مدفعاً». وأخرجه ابن عدي في جزء له عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً بلفظ : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات وأقيلوا الكرام عثراتهم إلا في حد من حدود الله»
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics

Giving them [the mu'allafatu] to ward off their evil when Islam was weak; but when Islam's power strengthened, the need to give them ceased. The Qur'an does not obligate giving to specific individuals by name from this share. (1) That does not mean that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) abrogated or suspended a commanding Qur'anic text; rather, he saw that those individuals who were considered mu'allafatu qulubuhum and to whom the Prophet gave this share from the treasury due to the need of Islam's call to win them over, support them, and reconcile their hearts, no longer needed Islam to purchase their support with money after Islam became established, and they became in need of being honored by it. Thus, he only withheld the gift from those specific individuals; as for the share of reconciliation and encouragement, its ruling in the Qur'an remains effective and has not ceased. If the need arises for the nation at some time or place to win hearts, mobilize forces to repel an enemy, or encourage those whom high virtues do not encourage as money does, then one gives from this share allocated for this need, similar to expenditures that states today allocate in their budgets for political propaganda. If the need for propaganda ceases in a place or among a group, it is not then permissible to spend money on those who do not need propaganda; rather, the money is saved in the state treasury until the need arises again. The nature of need may change from era to era, so giving to individuals who were once given due to their prestige and influence as in the past may become unnecessary, while it becomes necessary to give to newspaper owners, or establish propaganda magazines or a radio station, as in our time today. Imam Abu Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam said in his book 'Al-Amwal' regarding the conditions the Prophet (peace be upon him) set for Banu Thaqif, the people of Ta'if, when they entered Islam: 'In it, he set conditions for them at their conversion that were specific to them, unlike other people, such as declaring their valley inviolable, that no one should enter to overpower them, and that only someone from among them should be appointed as their ruler. This is what I told you: the Imam considers what is best for Islam and its people. If he fears an enemy's dominance that he cannot repel except by giving a gift to turn them back, he does as the Prophet did with the allied tribes on the day of the Trench. Similarly, if they refuse to accept Islam except upon the condition that he gives them something, and their acceptance brings strength to Islam, and he is not safe from their harm and violence, he gives them to reconcile their hearts, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did with the mu'allafatu qulubuhum until they desire Islam and their intention improves. This is only permissible as long as it does not involve violating the Book or the Sunnah. This is demonstrated by the fact that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not legalize usury for them in what he gave them. Do you not see that he stipulated that they would have their capital? (Al-Amwal, 1/193-194, first edition, Hijazi Press, Cairo.)

Chapter (11) The Second Stage: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

2- Also Umar's ijtihad in suspending the implementation of the hadd punishment for theft for thieves in the year of famine called 'Year of Ramada'; and his sufficing with ta'zir for the thief instead of cutting off his hand. This is not from Umar an abrogation of the legal hadd for theft, but rather a wise ijtihad from him in applying the conditions of this punishment, because among its legal conditions is that the thief should not be compelled to steal out of necessity. Umar considered in that a general doubt that they were stealing due to compelling necessity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts.' (1)

3- Also his allowing marriage for the wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance without waiting to confirm his death or the death of his peers - i.e., his age mates - as previously mentioned in the discussion of masalih mursalah. (See: F 12/5).

4- Also Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of the lands of Sawad of Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquering mujahideen who demanded their division among themselves as war booty is divided, after taking one-fifth, arguing with the apparent meanings of some Qur'anic verses and hadiths regarding the rights of mujahideen who gain ghanimah; they intended to consider the lands of the conquered countries as war booty to be distributed to the conquering army. Umar took a contrary opinion, considering the lands as fay', which concerns the rights of all Muslims, present and future, in consideration of the maslaha of future generations. (1) Narrated from 'Aisha, 'Ali, and Abu Hurairah. As for 'Aisha's hadith: Tirmidhi (1424) in Hudud: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if there is a way out, let them go, for the Imam to err in forgiveness is better than to err in punishment.' Weak in marfu' form. Al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak (4/384) said it is sahih al-isnad but Dhahabi criticized it. As for 'Ali's hadith: al-Daraqutni (3/84). As for Abu Hurairah's hadith: Ibn Majah (2545): 'Deflect the hudud as long as you find a way to deflect.' Also narrated by Ibn 'Adi in a part from Ibn 'Abbas marfu': 'Avert the hudud by doubts, and overlook the slips of the honorable except in a hadd of Allah.’
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a historical presentation of four examples of ijtihad by Caliph Umar (رضي الله عنه) during the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, the second stage of Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, is demonstrating how the early Muslims applied legal reasoning (ijtihad) to adapt rulings to changing circumstances without abrogating the primary texts.

The first example concerns the category of 'mu'allafatu qulubuhum' (those whose hearts are to be won over). Umar discontinued giving them from the zakah fund because, after Islam became strong, the need for buying their allegiance was gone. However, the author clarifies that this does not abrogate the Qur'anic ruling; the category remains, and if a similar need arises in the future (e.g., for political propaganda), the state may allocate funds for it. This is supported by a lengthy quote from Imam Abu Ubayd explaining that such concessions are permissible as long as they do not contradict the Book and Sunnah, as shown by the Prophet's treaty with Banu Thaqif.

The second example is Umar's suspension of the hadd amputation for theft during the famine year known as 'عام الرمادة'. Umar did not abolish the hadd; rather, he applied the rule that hudud are averted by doubts. Here, the general necessity (shubhah 'ammah) of stealing due to hunger created a doubt, so he used ta'zir (discretionary punishment) instead. This directly applies the hadith 'ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' (Avert the hudud by doubts).

The third example concerns the wife of a missing person. Umar allowed her to remarry after four years of the husband's disappearance, without waiting for proof of his death or the death of his peers. This ijtihad is based on maslaha mursalah (unrestricted public interest) to prevent hardship for the wife.

The fourth example is about the conquered lands of Iraq and Egypt. Some companions argued that these lands should be divided as ghanimah (war booty) among the soldiers. Umar disagreed, classifying them as fay' (property acquired without war) that belongs to the entire Muslim community, present and future, for the sake of intergenerational justice. This decision became the Hanafi position.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that Umar's ijtihad did not abrogate any text; it merely suspended or modified the application of rulings due to changing circumstances, guided by principles like maslaha, shubah, and the objectives of the Shariah.

Key Terms
المؤلفة قلوبهم (al-mu'allafatu qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are to be won over; a category eligible for zakah or state funds to attract them to Islam or prevent their harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Caliph Umar stop giving to the mu'allafatu qulubuhum?
A) He considered the Qur'anic verse abrogated by later revelation.
B) The need to purchase their allegiance ceased after Islam became strong.
C) He wanted to save money for military campaigns.
D) They refused to accept the money.

Key Takeaway
Umar's suspension of payments to mu'allafatu was a model of ijtihad that applies maslaha without abrogating the permanent text.

Suspension of Hadd for Theft During Famine
Second example of Umar's ijtihad with focus on shubhah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'Avert the hudud by doubts' to cases of general necessity, such as theft during famine, and distinguish between suspension and abrogation.

Core Idea
General necessity creates a doubt that averts the hadd punishment, replacing it with ta'zir.

During the severe famine known as 'Year of Ramada', Caliph Umar suspended the amputation penalty for thieves and instead used ta'zir (discretionary punishment). This was not an abolition of the hadd, but an application of the prophetic principle that hudud are averted by doubts. This lesson analyses the legal reasoning behind this ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its normal conditions
The hadd for theft requires the thief to steal without duress or necessity.
2
Understand Umar's application of the qawida
Umar considered the famine a general doubt (shubhah 'ammah) that created a presumption of necessity, thus averting the hadd.
3
Distinguish from abrogation
Umar did not repeal the hadd; he simply found the condition (no necessity) absent, so the hadd did not apply.
4
Apply the qawida to other scenarios
If a similar general doubt exists today, the same principle applies: ta'zir instead of hadd.

(1)

(الأموال، ۱۹۳/۱ - ١٩٤ الطبعة الأولى بمطبعة الحجازي بالقاهرة.

الفصل (۱۱) الدور الثاني : عصر الخلفاء الراشدين

۲ - وكذا اجتهاد عمر في وقف تنفيذ حد السرقة على السارقين في عام المجاعة المسمى: (عام) الرّمادة» ؛ واكتفائه بتعزير السارق بدلاً من قطع يده. وهذا من عمر ليس تعطيلاً لحد السرقة الشرعي، بل هو اجتهاد حكيم منه في تطبيق شرائط هذه العقوبة، لأنَّ من شرائطها شرعاً أن لا يكون السارق مضطراً إلى السرقة اضطراراً. فقد اعتبر عمر في ذلك شبهة عامة في أنهم كانوا يسرقون عن ضرورة ملجئة وقد قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات (۱) .

٣ - وكذلك إطلاقه النكاح لزوجة المفقود بعد أربع سنين من فقده دون انتظار تحقق وفاته أو موت لداته - أي أقرانه في العمر ـ كما سلف ذكره في بحث المصالح المرسلة. (ر: ف ١٢/٥).

٤ - وكذلك اجتهاد عمر في منع تقسيم أراضي سواد العراق وأراضي مصر على المجاهدين الفاتحين لها الذين طالبوا بتقسيمها بينهم كما تقسم الغنائم الحربية بعد تخميسها محتجين بظواهر بعض نصوص القرآن والسنة في حقوق المجاهدين الغانمين في الغنيمة ؛ فإنهم أرادوا اعتبار أراضي البلاد المفتوحة كالغنائم الحربية توزع على الجيش الفاتح.
وقد ذهب عمر إلى خلاف رأيهم، فاعتبر الأراضي من الفيء الذي تتعلق به حقوق المسلمين عامة حاضرهم وآتيهم، رعاية لمصلحة الأجيال
(۱) روي من حديث عائشة، ومن حديث علي ومن حديث أبي هريرة، أما حديث عائشة فأخرجه الترمذي (١٤٢٤) في الحدود، بلفظ: ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم فإن كان له مخرج فخلوا سبيله، فإن الإمام أن يخطىء في العفو خير من يخطىء في العقوبة وضعف رفعه وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك ٣٨٤/٤، وقال: صحيح الإسناد، وتعقبه الذهبي. وأما حديث علي فأخرجه الدارقطني ٨٤/٣ وأما حديث أبي هريرة فأخرجه ابن ماجه (٢٥٤٥) بلفظ : ادفعوا الحدود ما وجدتم له مدفعاً». وأخرجه ابن عدي في جزء له عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً بلفظ : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات وأقيلوا الكرام عثراتهم إلا في حد من حدود الله»
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics

Giving them [the mu'allafatu] to ward off their evil when Islam was weak; but when Islam's power strengthened, the need to give them ceased. The Qur'an does not obligate giving to specific individuals by name from this share. (1) That does not mean that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) abrogated or suspended a commanding Qur'anic text; rather, he saw that those individuals who were considered mu'allafatu qulubuhum and to whom the Prophet gave this share from the treasury due to the need of Islam's call to win them over, support them, and reconcile their hearts, no longer needed Islam to purchase their support with money after Islam became established, and they became in need of being honored by it. Thus, he only withheld the gift from those specific individuals; as for the share of reconciliation and encouragement, its ruling in the Qur'an remains effective and has not ceased. If the need arises for the nation at some time or place to win hearts, mobilize forces to repel an enemy, or encourage those whom high virtues do not encourage as money does, then one gives from this share allocated for this need, similar to expenditures that states today allocate in their budgets for political propaganda. If the need for propaganda ceases in a place or among a group, it is not then permissible to spend money on those who do not need propaganda; rather, the money is saved in the state treasury until the need arises again. The nature of need may change from era to era, so giving to individuals who were once given due to their prestige and influence as in the past may become unnecessary, while it becomes necessary to give to newspaper owners, or establish propaganda magazines or a radio station, as in our time today. Imam Abu Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam said in his book 'Al-Amwal' regarding the conditions the Prophet (peace be upon him) set for Banu Thaqif, the people of Ta'if, when they entered Islam: 'In it, he set conditions for them at their conversion that were specific to them, unlike other people, such as declaring their valley inviolable, that no one should enter to overpower them, and that only someone from among them should be appointed as their ruler. This is what I told you: the Imam considers what is best for Islam and its people. If he fears an enemy's dominance that he cannot repel except by giving a gift to turn them back, he does as the Prophet did with the allied tribes on the day of the Trench. Similarly, if they refuse to accept Islam except upon the condition that he gives them something, and their acceptance brings strength to Islam, and he is not safe from their harm and violence, he gives them to reconcile their hearts, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did with the mu'allafatu qulubuhum until they desire Islam and their intention improves. This is only permissible as long as it does not involve violating the Book or the Sunnah. This is demonstrated by the fact that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not legalize usury for them in what he gave them. Do you not see that he stipulated that they would have their capital? (Al-Amwal, 1/193-194, first edition, Hijazi Press, Cairo.)

Chapter (11) The Second Stage: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

2- Also Umar's ijtihad in suspending the implementation of the hadd punishment for theft for thieves in the year of famine called 'Year of Ramada'; and his sufficing with ta'zir for the thief instead of cutting off his hand. This is not from Umar an abrogation of the legal hadd for theft, but rather a wise ijtihad from him in applying the conditions of this punishment, because among its legal conditions is that the thief should not be compelled to steal out of necessity. Umar considered in that a general doubt that they were stealing due to compelling necessity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts.' (1)

3- Also his allowing marriage for the wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance without waiting to confirm his death or the death of his peers - i.e., his age mates - as previously mentioned in the discussion of masalih mursalah. (See: F 12/5).

4- Also Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of the lands of Sawad of Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquering mujahideen who demanded their division among themselves as war booty is divided, after taking one-fifth, arguing with the apparent meanings of some Qur'anic verses and hadiths regarding the rights of mujahideen who gain ghanimah; they intended to consider the lands of the conquered countries as war booty to be distributed to the conquering army. Umar took a contrary opinion, considering the lands as fay', which concerns the rights of all Muslims, present and future, in consideration of the maslaha of future generations. (1) Narrated from 'Aisha, 'Ali, and Abu Hurairah. As for 'Aisha's hadith: Tirmidhi (1424) in Hudud: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if there is a way out, let them go, for the Imam to err in forgiveness is better than to err in punishment.' Weak in marfu' form. Al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak (4/384) said it is sahih al-isnad but Dhahabi criticized it. As for 'Ali's hadith: al-Daraqutni (3/84). As for Abu Hurairah's hadith: Ibn Majah (2545): 'Deflect the hudud as long as you find a way to deflect.' Also narrated by Ibn 'Adi in a part from Ibn 'Abbas marfu': 'Avert the hudud by doubts, and overlook the slips of the honorable except in a hadd of Allah.’

Commentary
This passage is a historical presentation of four examples of ijtihad by Caliph Umar (رضي الله عنه) during the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, the second stage of Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, is demonstrating how the early Muslims applied legal reasoning (ijtihad) to adapt rulings to changing circumstances without abrogating the primary texts.

The first example concerns the category of 'mu'allafatu qulubuhum' (those whose hearts are to be won over). Umar discontinued giving them from the zakah fund because, after Islam became strong, the need for buying their allegiance was gone. However, the author clarifies that this does not abrogate the Qur'anic ruling; the category remains, and if a similar need arises in the future (e.g., for political propaganda), the state may allocate funds for it. This is supported by a lengthy quote from Imam Abu Ubayd explaining that such concessions are permissible as long as they do not contradict the Book and Sunnah, as shown by the Prophet's treaty with Banu Thaqif.

The second example is Umar's suspension of the hadd amputation for theft during the famine year known as 'عام الرمادة'. Umar did not abolish the hadd; rather, he applied the rule that hudud are averted by doubts. Here, the general necessity (shubhah 'ammah) of stealing due to hunger created a doubt, so he used ta'zir (discretionary punishment) instead. This directly applies the hadith 'ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' (Avert the hudud by doubts).

The third example concerns the wife of a missing person. Umar allowed her to remarry after four years of the husband's disappearance, without waiting for proof of his death or the death of his peers. Umar disagreed, classifying them as fay' (property acquired without war) that belongs to the entire Muslim community, present and future, for the sake of intergenerational justice. This decision became the Hanafi position.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that Umar's ijtihad did not abrogate any text; it merely suspended or modified the application of rulings due to changing circumstances, guided by principles like maslaha, shubah, and the objectives of the Shariah.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Umar do instead of amputating the hands of thieves during the famine?

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Remarriage of Wife of Missing Person
Third example of Umar's ijtihad: maslaha mursalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Umar's ijtihad allowing a wife to remarry after four years of her husband's disappearance as an application of maslaha mursalah, and identify the conditions for such a ruling.

Core Idea
Maslaha permitted a time limit to prevent hardship for the wife of a missing husband.

In the case of a missing husband, Umar ruled that the wife could remarry after four years without waiting for confirmation of death. This was not based on a specific text but on the principle of maslaha mursalah (unrestricted public interest) to alleviate the wife's prolonged suffering. This lesson explores the legal reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
A wife whose husband is missing faces indefinite uncertainty; she cannot remarry and may suffer hardship.
2
Understand Umar's solution
Umar set a four-year waiting period, after which the wife could remarry, treating the husband as likely dead.
3
Identify the basis: maslaha mursalah
There is no explicit text for this specific period; Umar used ijtihad based on maslaha to prevent harm.
4
Distinguish from other rules
This is not the same as the 'iddah of divorce or death; it is a new ruling for a unique situation.

ليس معنى ذلك أن عمر رضي الله عنه قد أبطل أو عطل نصاً قرآنياً آمراً، ولكنه رأى أن أولئك الأشخاص الذين كانوا يعدون من المؤلفة قلوبهم ويعطيهم النبي هذا السهم من بيت المال لاحتياج دعوة الإسلام إلى ترغيبهم وتأييدهم وتأليف قلوبهم لم يبق للإسلام حاجة في شراء تأييدهم بالمال بعد أن استتب له الأمر، وأصبحوا هم في حاجة إلى الاعتزاز به فهو إنما حَبَس العطاء عن هؤلاء الأشخاص بأعيانهم؛ أما سهم التأليف والترغيب فحكمه في القرآن باق لم يتوقف فإذا تجددت للأمة حاجة في زمان أو مكان آخر إلى تأليف القلوب، أو تأليب القوى لدفع عدو، أو ترغيب من لا ترغبه الفضائل السامية كما يرغبه المال، فإنه يعطى من هذا السهم المرصود لهذه الحاجة كشأن النفقات التي تخصصها الدول اليوم في ميزانياتها لأجل الدعاية السياسية. فإذا انقضت حاجة الدعاية في مكان أو في فئة لا يسوغ عندئذ صرف المال إلى من لا تحتاج الدعاية إليه، بل يوفّر المال على خزينة الدولة ريثما تتجدد الحاجة. وقد يتغير وجه الحاجة بين عصر وعصر، فيستغنى عن إعطاء أشخاص كانوا يعطون لوجاهتهم ونفوذهم كما كان في الماضي، ويحتاج إلى إعطاء أرباب الصحف، أو إنشاء مجلات دعاية أو محطة إذاعة لاسلكية، كما في عصرنا اليوم. قال الإمام أبو عبيد القاسم بن سلام في كتابه «الأموال» بمناسبة ما اشترطه النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لبني ثقيف أهل الطائف عند دخولهم في الإسلام ما نصه: «وفيه أنه شرط لهم شروطاً عند إسلامهم خاصة لهم دون الناس مثل تحريمه واديهم، وأن لا يدخله أحد يغلبهم عليه، وأن لا يؤمر عليهم إلا بعضهم. وهذا مما قلت لك : إنّ الإمام ناظر للإسلام وأهله فإذا خاف من عدو غلبة لا يقدر على دفعهم إلا بعطية يردهم بها فعل كالذي صنع النبي له بالأحزاب يوم الخندق . وكذلك لو أبوا أن يسلموا إلا على شيء يجعله لهم، وكان في إسلامهم عز للإسلام، ولم يأمن معرتهم وبأسهم أعطاهم ذلك ليتألفهم به كما فعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالمؤلفة قلوبهم إلى أن يرغبوا في الإسلام، وتحسن فيه نيتهم. وإنما يجوز من هذا ما لم يكن فيه نقض للكتاب ولا للسنة. ويبين ذلك أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يجعل لهم فيما أعطاهم تحليل الربا، ألا تراه قد شَرَط عليهم أن لهم رؤوس أموالهم؟). (الأموال، ۱۹۳/۱ - ١٩٤ الطبعة الأولى بمطبعة الحجازي بالقاهرة.

الفصل (۱۱) الدور الثاني : عصر الخلفاء الراشدين

۲ - وكذا اجتهاد عمر في وقف تنفيذ حد السرقة على السارقين في عام المجاعة المسمى: (عام) الرّمادة» ؛ واكتفائه بتعزير السارق بدلاً من قطع يده. وهذا من عمر ليس تعطيلاً لحد السرقة الشرعي، بل هو اجتهاد حكيم منه في تطبيق شرائط هذه العقوبة، لأنَّ من شرائطها شرعاً أن لا يكون السارق مضطراً إلى السرقة اضطراراً. فقد اعتبر عمر في ذلك شبهة عامة في أنهم كانوا يسرقون عن ضرورة ملجئة وقد قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات (۱) .

٣ - وكذلك إطلاقه النكاح لزوجة المفقود بعد أربع سنين من فقده دون انتظار تحقق وفاته أو موت لداته - أي أقرانه في العمر ـ كما سلف ذكره في بحث المصالح المرسلة. (ر: ف ١٢/٥).

٤ - وكذلك اجتهاد عمر في منع تقسيم أراضي سواد العراق وأراضي مصر على المجاهدين الفاتحين لها الذين طالبوا بتقسيمها بينهم كما تقسم الغنائم الحربية بعد تخميسها محتجين بظواهر بعض نصوص القرآن والسنة في حقوق المجاهدين الغانمين في الغنيمة ؛ فإنهم أرادوا اعتبار أراضي البلاد المفتوحة كالغنائم الحربية توزع على الجيش الفاتح.
وقد ذهب عمر إلى خلاف رأيهم، فاعتبر الأراضي من الفيء الذي تتعلق به حقوق المسلمين عامة حاضرهم وآتيهم، رعاية لمصلحة الأجيال
(۱) روي من حديث عائشة، ومن حديث علي ومن حديث أبي هريرة، أما حديث عائشة فأخرجه الترمذي (١٤٢٤) في الحدود، بلفظ: ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم فإن كان له مخرج فخلوا سبيله، فإن الإمام أن يخطىء في العفو خير من يخطىء في العقوبة وضعف رفعه وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك ٣٨٤/٤، وقال: صحيح الإسناد، وتعقبه الذهبي. وأما حديث علي فأخرجه الدارقطني ٨٤/٣ وأما حديث أبي هريرة فأخرجه ابن ماجه (٢٥٤٥) بلفظ : ادفعوا الحدود ما وجدتم له مدفعاً». وأخرجه ابن عدي في جزء له عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً بلفظ : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات وأقيلوا الكرام عثراتهم إلا في حد من حدود الله»
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics

Giving them [the mu'allafatu] to ward off their evil when Islam was weak; but when Islam's power strengthened, the need to give them ceased. The Qur'an does not obligate giving to specific individuals by name from this share. (1) That does not mean that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) abrogated or suspended a commanding Qur'anic text; rather, he saw that those individuals who were considered mu'allafatu qulubuhum and to whom the Prophet gave this share from the treasury due to the need of Islam's call to win them over, support them, and reconcile their hearts, no longer needed Islam to purchase their support with money after Islam became established, and they became in need of being honored by it. Thus, he only withheld the gift from those specific individuals; as for the share of reconciliation and encouragement, its ruling in the Qur'an remains effective and has not ceased. If the need arises for the nation at some time or place to win hearts, mobilize forces to repel an enemy, or encourage those whom high virtues do not encourage as money does, then one gives from this share allocated for this need, similar to expenditures that states today allocate in their budgets for political propaganda. If the need for propaganda ceases in a place or among a group, it is not then permissible to spend money on those who do not need propaganda; rather, the money is saved in the state treasury until the need arises again. The nature of need may change from era to era, so giving to individuals who were once given due to their prestige and influence as in the past may become unnecessary, while it becomes necessary to give to newspaper owners, or establish propaganda magazines or a radio station, as in our time today. Imam Abu Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam said in his book 'Al-Amwal' regarding the conditions the Prophet (peace be upon him) set for Banu Thaqif, the people of Ta'if, when they entered Islam: 'In it, he set conditions for them at their conversion that were specific to them, unlike other people, such as declaring their valley inviolable, that no one should enter to overpower them, and that only someone from among them should be appointed as their ruler. This is what I told you: the Imam considers what is best for Islam and its people. If he fears an enemy's dominance that he cannot repel except by giving a gift to turn them back, he does as the Prophet did with the allied tribes on the day of the Trench. Similarly, if they refuse to accept Islam except upon the condition that he gives them something, and their acceptance brings strength to Islam, and he is not safe from their harm and violence, he gives them to reconcile their hearts, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did with the mu'allafatu qulubuhum until they desire Islam and their intention improves. This is only permissible as long as it does not involve violating the Book or the Sunnah. This is demonstrated by the fact that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not legalize usury for them in what he gave them. Do you not see that he stipulated that they would have their capital? (Al-Amwal, 1/193-194, first edition, Hijazi Press, Cairo.)

Chapter (11) The Second Stage: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

2- Also Umar's ijtihad in suspending the implementation of the hadd punishment for theft for thieves in the year of famine called 'Year of Ramada'; and his sufficing with ta'zir for the thief instead of cutting off his hand. This is not from Umar an abrogation of the legal hadd for theft, but rather a wise ijtihad from him in applying the conditions of this punishment, because among its legal conditions is that the thief should not be compelled to steal out of necessity. Umar considered in that a general doubt that they were stealing due to compelling necessity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts.' (1)

3- Also his allowing marriage for the wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance without waiting to confirm his death or the death of his peers - i.e., his age mates - as previously mentioned in the discussion of masalih mursalah. (See: F 12/5).

4- Also Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of the lands of Sawad of Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquering mujahideen who demanded their division among themselves as war booty is divided, after taking one-fifth, arguing with the apparent meanings of some Qur'anic verses and hadiths regarding the rights of mujahideen who gain ghanimah; they intended to consider the lands of the conquered countries as war booty to be distributed to the conquering army. Umar took a contrary opinion, considering the lands as fay', which concerns the rights of all Muslims, present and future, in consideration of the maslaha of future generations. (1) Narrated from 'Aisha, 'Ali, and Abu Hurairah. As for 'Aisha's hadith: Tirmidhi (1424) in Hudud: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if there is a way out, let them go, for the Imam to err in forgiveness is better than to err in punishment.' Weak in marfu' form. Al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak (4/384) said it is sahih al-isnad but Dhahabi criticized it. As for 'Ali's hadith: al-Daraqutni (3/84). As for Abu Hurairah's hadith: Ibn Majah (2545): 'Deflect the hudud as long as you find a way to deflect.' Also narrated by Ibn 'Adi in a part from Ibn 'Abbas marfu': 'Avert the hudud by doubts, and overlook the slips of the honorable except in a hadd of Allah.’
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a historical presentation of four examples of ijtihad by Caliph Umar (رضي الله عنه) during the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, the second stage of Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, is demonstrating how the early Muslims applied legal reasoning (ijtihad) to adapt rulings to changing circumstances without abrogating the primary texts.

The first example concerns the category of 'mu'allafatu qulubuhum' (those whose hearts are to be won over). Umar discontinued giving them from the zakah fund because, after Islam became strong, the need for buying their allegiance was gone. However, the author clarifies that this does not abrogate the Qur'anic ruling; the category remains, and if a similar need arises in the future (e.g., for political propaganda), the state may allocate funds for it. This is supported by a lengthy quote from Imam Abu Ubayd explaining that such concessions are permissible as long as they do not contradict the Book and Sunnah, as shown by the Prophet's treaty with Banu Thaqif.

The second example is Umar's suspension of the hadd amputation for theft during the famine year known as 'عام الرمادة'. Umar did not abolish the hadd; rather, he applied the rule that hudud are averted by doubts. Here, the general necessity (shubhah 'ammah) of stealing due to hunger created a doubt, so he used ta'zir (discretionary punishment) instead. This directly applies the hadith 'ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' (Avert the hudud by doubts).

The third example concerns the wife of a missing person. Umar allowed her to remarry after four years of the husband's disappearance, without waiting for proof of his death or the death of his peers. This ijtihad is based on maslaha mursalah (unrestricted public interest) to prevent hardship for the wife.

The fourth example is about the conquered lands of Iraq and Egypt. Some companions argued that these lands should be divided as ghanimah (war booty) among the soldiers. Umar disagreed, classifying them as fay' (property acquired without war) that belongs to the entire Muslim community, present and future, for the sake of intergenerational justice. This decision became the Hanafi position.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that Umar's ijtihad did not abrogate any text; it merely suspended or modified the application of rulings due to changing circumstances, guided by principles like maslaha, shubah, and the objectives of the Shariah.

Key Terms
المؤلفة قلوبهم (al-mu'allafatu qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are to be won over; a category eligible for zakah or state funds to attract them to Islam or prevent their harm.
الفيء (al-fay'): Property acquired from non-Muslims without war, considered communal property for all Muslims, not divided as spoils.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On what basis did Umar allow the wife of a missing person to remarry after four years?
A) A specific Quranic verse.
B) The principle of maslaha mursalah.
C) Analogy to divorce.
D) A hadith from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Umar's ijtihad on the wife of a missing person exemplifies how maslaha can generate rulings for unprecedented cases.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Conquered Lands as Fay'
Fourth example: Umar's ijtihad on fay' vs ghanimah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi distinction between ghanimah and fay' and evaluate Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of conquered lands for intergenerational maslaha.

Core Idea
Fay' belongs to all Muslims, present and future; it is not war booty to be divided among soldiers.

When the lands of Iraq and Egypt were conquered, some companions demanded they be divided as ghanimah. Caliph Umar disagreed and classified them as fay', which is communal property for all Muslims. This lesson explores the legal distinction and the maslaha behind Umar's decision, which became the standard Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories: ghanimah and fay'
Ghanimah is war booty taken by force, divided after taking one-fifth. Fay' is property acquired without war (e.g., peace treaty), belonging to the entire community.
2
Identify Umar's classification
Umar considered the conquered lands as fay', not ghanimah, to preserve them for future generations.
3
Understand the ijtihad
Despite the apparent generality of some texts that treat conquered lands as booty, Umar used maslaha to reclassify them, considering the long-term benefit.
4
Trace the Hanafi adoption
The Hanafi school follows Umar's view, making it a foundational principle of Islamic fiscal policy.

Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics

Giving them [the mu'allafatu] to ward off their evil when Islam was weak; but when Islam's power strengthened, the need to give them ceased. The Qur'an does not obligate giving to specific individuals by name from this share. (1) That does not mean that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) abrogated or suspended a commanding Qur'anic text; rather, he saw that those individuals who were considered mu'allafatu qulubuhum and to whom the Prophet gave this share from the treasury due to the need of Islam's call to win them over, support them, and reconcile their hearts, no longer needed Islam to purchase their support with money after Islam became established, and they became in need of being honored by it. Thus, he only withheld the gift from those specific individuals; as for the share of reconciliation and encouragement, its ruling in the Qur'an remains effective and has not ceased. If the need arises for the nation at some time or place to win hearts, mobilize forces to repel an enemy, or encourage those whom high virtues do not encourage as money does, then one gives from this share allocated for this need, similar to expenditures that states today allocate in their budgets for political propaganda. If the need for propaganda ceases in a place or among a group, it is not then permissible to spend money on those who do not need propaganda; rather, the money is saved in the state treasury until the need arises again. The nature of need may change from era to era, so giving to individuals who were once given due to their prestige and influence as in the past may become unnecessary, while it becomes necessary to give to newspaper owners, or establish propaganda magazines or a radio station, as in our time today. Imam Abu Ubayd al-Qasim ibn Sallam said in his book 'Al-Amwal' regarding the conditions the Prophet (peace be upon him) set for Banu Thaqif, the people of Ta'if, when they entered Islam: 'In it, he set conditions for them at their conversion that were specific to them, unlike other people, such as declaring their valley inviolable, that no one should enter to overpower them, and that only someone from among them should be appointed as their ruler. This is what I told you: the Imam considers what is best for Islam and its people. If he fears an enemy's dominance that he cannot repel except by giving a gift to turn them back, he does as the Prophet did with the allied tribes on the day of the Trench. Similarly, if they refuse to accept Islam except upon the condition that he gives them something, and their acceptance brings strength to Islam, and he is not safe from their harm and violence, he gives them to reconcile their hearts, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did with the mu'allafatu qulubuhum until they desire Islam and their intention improves. This is only permissible as long as it does not involve violating the Book or the Sunnah. This is demonstrated by the fact that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not legalize usury for them in what he gave them. Do you not see that he stipulated that they would have their capital? (Al-Amwal, 1/193-194, first edition, Hijazi Press, Cairo.)

Chapter (11) The Second Stage: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

2- Also Umar's ijtihad in suspending the implementation of the hadd punishment for theft for thieves in the year of famine called 'Year of Ramada'; and his sufficing with ta'zir for the thief instead of cutting off his hand. This is not from Umar an abrogation of the legal hadd for theft, but rather a wise ijtihad from him in applying the conditions of this punishment, because among its legal conditions is that the thief should not be compelled to steal out of necessity. Umar considered in that a general doubt that they were stealing due to compelling necessity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts.' (1)

3- Also his allowing marriage for the wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance without waiting to confirm his death or the death of his peers - i.e., his age mates - as previously mentioned in the discussion of masalih mursalah. (See: F 12/5).

4- Also Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of the lands of Sawad of Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquering mujahideen who demanded their division among themselves as war booty is divided, after taking one-fifth, arguing with the apparent meanings of some Qur'anic verses and hadiths regarding the rights of mujahideen who gain ghanimah; they intended to consider the lands of the conquered countries as war booty to be distributed to the conquering army. Umar took a contrary opinion, considering the lands as fay', which concerns the rights of all Muslims, present and future, in consideration of the maslaha of future generations. (1) Narrated from 'Aisha, 'Ali, and Abu Hurairah. As for 'Aisha's hadith: Tirmidhi (1424) in Hudud: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if there is a way out, let them go, for the Imam to err in forgiveness is better than to err in punishment.' Weak in marfu' form. Al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak (4/384) said it is sahih al-isnad but Dhahabi criticized it. As for 'Ali's hadith: al-Daraqutni (3/84). As for Abu Hurairah's hadith: Ibn Majah (2545): 'Deflect the hudud as long as you find a way to deflect.' Also narrated by Ibn 'Adi in a part from Ibn 'Abbas marfu': 'Avert the hudud by doubts, and overlook the slips of the honorable except in a hadd of Allah.’
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a historical presentation of four examples of ijtihad by Caliph Umar (رضي الله عنه) during the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, the second stage of Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, is demonstrating how the early Muslims applied legal reasoning (ijtihad) to adapt rulings to changing circumstances without abrogating the primary texts.

The first example concerns the category of 'mu'allafatu qulubuhum' (those whose hearts are to be won over). Umar discontinued giving them from the zakah fund because, after Islam became strong, the need for buying their allegiance was gone. However, the author clarifies that this does not abrogate the Qur'anic ruling; the category remains, and if a similar need arises in the future (e.g., for political propaganda), the state may allocate funds for it. This is supported by a lengthy quote from Imam Abu Ubayd explaining that such concessions are permissible as long as they do not contradict the Book and Sunnah, as shown by the Prophet's treaty with Banu Thaqif.

The second example is Umar's suspension of the hadd amputation for theft during the famine year known as 'عام الرمادة'. Umar did not abolish the hadd; rather, he applied the rule that hudud are averted by doubts. Here, the general necessity (shubhah 'ammah) of stealing due to hunger created a doubt, so he used ta'zir (discretionary punishment) instead. This directly applies the hadith 'ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات' (Avert the hudud by doubts).

The third example concerns the wife of a missing person. Umar allowed her to remarry after four years of the husband's disappearance, without waiting for proof of his death or the death of his peers. This ijtihad is based on maslaha mursalah (unrestricted public interest) to prevent hardship for the wife.

The fourth example is about the conquered lands of Iraq and Egypt. Some companions argued that these lands should be divided as ghanimah (war booty) among the soldiers. Umar disagreed, classifying them as fay' (property acquired without war) that belongs to the entire Muslim community, present and future, for the sake of intergenerational justice. This decision became the Hanafi position.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that Umar's ijtihad did not abrogate any text; it merely suspended or modified the application of rulings due to changing circumstances, guided by principles like maslaha, shubah, and the objectives of the Shariah.

Key Terms
الفيء (al-fay'): Property acquired from non-Muslims without war, considered communal property for all Muslims, not divided as spoils.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Umar refuse to divide the conquered lands of Iraq and Egypt among the soldiers?
A) He wanted to keep the land for himself.
B) He considered them fay' belonging to all Muslims, present and future.
C) He did not have enough soldiers to hold the land.
D) The land was not fertile.

Key Takeaway
Umar's classification of conquered lands as fay' is a landmark ijtihad that balances textual interpretation with communal maslaha.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Umar's Ijtihad: Suspending Hadd During Famine
Analysis of Umar's ijtihad on hadd suspension during famine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Umar's suspension of the hadd for theft during famine is a valid application of the shubha principle and identify the Hanafi acceptance of this reasoning.

Core Idea
Umar did not abolish the hadd; he applied its condition that necessity creates a shubha preventing the punishment.

In the severe famine known as 'Year of Ramadah,' thieves stole out of desperation. Caliph Umar refrained from amputating their hands, instead imposing a discretionary punishment. This decision is often misunderstood as suspending divine law, but it was a precise application of the Prophetic instruction to avert hudud by doubts. This lesson analyzes the legal reasoning behind Umar's ijtihad and its significance for the Hanafi school's approach to shubha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Recognize the context: a severe famine where people stole out of extreme necessity, not criminal intent. Umar's response was to impose ta'zir instead of the hadd punishment.
The hadd for theft is amputation of the hand, but only when conditions are met.
2
State the legal issue
The issue is whether the hadd can be applied when the thief is compelled by necessity. The Sharia conditions for hadd include the absence of genuine need or duress.
Necessity creates a shubha (doubt) regarding the criminal intent.
3
Identify the primary dalil
Umar relied on the hadith 'Avert the hudud by doubts' (ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات). This hadith, though with some weakness in chain, is accepted by Hanafi jurists as a basis for waiving hadd when doubt exists.
The hadith is narrated from multiple companions, strengthening its acceptance in practice.
4
Analyze Umar's application of the dalil
Umar considered the widespread famine as a general shubha affecting all thieves, not just individuals. This collective necessity created doubt that prevented the application of hadd. Thus, he substituted ta'zir, which is a discretionary punishment.
This shows ijtihad based on 'illa (effective cause) and public welfare.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul
The Hanafi school recognizes shubha as a preventing factor (mani') for hadd punishments. Umar's precedent is a key support for this principle, as the school traces its methodology to the early caliphs, especially Umar.
This example is often cited in Hanafi texts on hudud to illustrate the application of shubha.

الفصل (۱۱) الدور الثاني : عصر الخلفاء الراشدين
۲ - وكذا اجتهاد عمر في وقف تنفيذ حد السرقة على السارقين في عام المجاعة المسمى: (عام الرمادة)؛ واكتفائه بتعزير السارق بدلاً من قطع يده. وهذا من عمر ليس تعطيلاً لحد السرقة الشرعي، بل هو اجتهاد حكيم منه في تطبيق شرائط هذه العقوبة، لأنَّ من شرائطها شرعاً أن لا يكون السارق مضطراً إلى السرقة اضطراراً. فقد اعتبر عمر في ذلك شبهة عامة في أنهم كانوا يسرقون عن ضرورة ملجئة وقد قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات (۱) .
٣ - وكذلك إطلاقه النكاح لزوجة المفقود بعد أربع سنين من فقده دون انتظار تحقق وفاته أو موت لداته - أي أقرانه في العمر ـ كما سلف ذكره في بحث المصالح المرسلة. (ر: ف ١٢/٥).
٤ ـ وكذلك اجتهاد عمر في منع تقسيم أراضي سواد العراق وأراضي مصر على المجاهدين الفاتحين لها الذين طالبوا بتقسيمها بينهم كما تقسم الغنائم الحربية، بعد تخميسها محتجين بظواهر بعض نصوص القرآن والسنة في حقوق المجاهدين الغانمين في الغنيمة؛ فإنهم أرادوا اعتبار أراضي البلاد المفتوحة كالغنائم الحربية توزع على الجيش الفاتح.
وقد ذهب عمر إلى خلاف رأيهم، فاعتبر الأراضي من الفيء الذي تتعلق به حقوق المسلمين عامة حاضرهم وآتيهم، رعاية لمصلحة الأجيال وحقوقها في بيت المال، وفقاً لما ينبيء به النظر السديد إلى مجموع النصوص القرآنية لا إلى بعضها دون بعض.
فأبقى عمر الأرضين لأهليها، وطرح عليها ضريبة الخراج؛ لأن ذلك أصلح لإحيائها وأعم وأدوم لنفعها وريعها (1).
هذا، وإن عمر هو أول أنشأ منصب القضاء مستقلاً من عن غيره من الأعمال، وكان قبل ذلك تابعاً لولاية الأمراء والعمال، ومن جملة وظائفهم.
وإن طريقة عمر الفقهية كانت حجة خالدة لمدرسة «أهل الرأي» في العراق من فقهاء الطبقات التالية التي سيأتي ذكرها فقد تأثر هؤلاء بفقه ابن مسعود في الكوفة، وكان هو يسير على طريقة عمر رضي الله عنهما.
(۱) روي من حديث عائشة، ومن حديث علي ومن حديث أبي هريرة، أما حديث عائشة فأخرجه الترمذي (١٤٢٤) في الحدود، بلفظ: ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم فإن كان له مخرج فخلوا سبيله، فإن الإمام أن يخطىء في العفو خير من يخطىء في العقوبة وضعف رفعه وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك ٣٨٤/٤، وقال: صحيح الإسناد، وتعقبه الذهبي. وأما حديث علي فأخرجه الدارقطني ٨٤/٣ وأما حديث أبي هريرة فأخرجه ابن ماجه (٢٥٤٥) بلفظ : ادفعوا الحدود ما وجدتم له مدفعاً». وأخرجه ابن عدي في جزء له عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً بلفظ : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات وأقيلوا الكرام عثراتهم إلا في حد من حدود الله» (ر:ف ٤/٤٩).
(۱) فصلنا اجتهاد عمر في شأن الأراضي المفتوحة في الملحق بهذا الفصل.
Chapter (11) Second Cycle: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs
2 - Likewise, Umar's ijtihad in suspending the execution of the hadd punishment for theft on thieves in the year of famine called (Year of Ramadah); and his sufficing with tazir (discretionary punishment) for the thief instead of cutting off his hand. This from Umar is not a suspension of the legal hadd for theft, but is a wise ijtihad from him in applying the conditions of this punishment, because among its conditions legally is that the thief not be compelled to theft by necessity. So Umar considered in that a general doubt (shubha) that they were stealing due to compelling necessity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts (shubuhat).' (1)
3 - Likewise, his permitting marriage for the wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance without waiting for verification of his death or the death of his peers - i.e., his age mates - as previously mentioned in the discussion of considerations of public interest (masalih mursalah). (See: f 12/5).
4 - Likewise, Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of the lands of Sawad of Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquering mujahideen who demanded their division among themselves as war booty is divided, after taking one-fifth, arguing with the apparent meanings of some Quranic and Sunnah texts regarding the rights of mujahideen to spoils; for they intended to consider the lands of the conquered territories as war booty distributed to the conquering army.
And Umar went contrary to their opinion, and he considered the lands as fay' (public property) to which the rights of Muslims in general, present and future, are attached, taking care of the interest of generations and their rights in the treasury, according to what sound consideration of the totality of Quranic texts indicates, not some of them without others.
So Umar left the lands to their owners, and imposed upon them the kharaj tax; because that was more beneficial for their cultivation and more comprehensive and lasting for their benefit and yield (1).
Moreover, Umar was the first to establish the position of judiciary independently from other offices, whereas before it was subordinate to the governors and officials, and part of their functions.
And indeed, Umar's jurisprudential method was an enduring proof for the school of 'Ahl al-Ra'y' in Iraq among the jurists of the following generations who will be mentioned later; for these were influenced by the fiqh of Ibn Mas'ud in Kufa, and he followed the method of Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both).
(1) Narrated from the hadith of Aisha, and from the hadith of Ali, and from the hadith of Abu Hurairah. As for the hadith of Aisha, it is recorded by al-Tirmidhi (1424) in the Book of Hudud with the wording: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if there is a way out, let him go, for the Imam to err in pardon is better than to err in punishment.' And he considered its raising (to the Prophet) weak. Al-Hakim recorded it in al-Mustadrak (4/384) and said: 'The chain is authentic,' but al-Dhahabi criticized it. As for the hadith of Ali, it is recorded by al-Daraqutni (3/84). As for the hadith of Abu Hurairah, it is recorded by Ibn Majah (2545) with the wording: 'Avert the hudud as long as you find a way to avert them.' And Ibn Adi recorded in a part of his work from Ibn Abbas with a raised chain: 'Avert the hudud by doubts and overlook the slips of the noble except in a hadd from the limits of Allah.' (See: f 4/49).
(1) We have detailed Umar's ijtihad regarding the conquered lands in the appendix to this chapter.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work presents a historical and legal analysis of three famous ijtihadat of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA). The author classifies this as part of the 'second cycle' of Islamic jurisprudence: the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The passage serves to illustrate how early juristic reasoning (ijtihad) operated within the framework of Sharia by applying general principles and conditions rather than merely following literal texts.

The first example concerns the suspension of the hadd punishment for theft during the severe famine known as 'Year of Ramadah.' Umar refrained from amputating thieves' hands and instead imposed a lesser discretionary punishment (ta'zir). The author clarifies that this was not an abandonment of the divine penalty but a proper application of its conditions: one condition is that the thief not be compelled by necessity. Umar considered the widespread famine as creating a general doubt (shubha) about criminal intent, relying on the Prophetic instruction to avert hudud by doubts. This demonstrates ijtihad based on textual evidence (the hadith) and causal reasoning ('illa).

The second example is Umar's ruling on the wife of a missing person. He allowed her to remarry after four years of waiting, without requiring proof of death or waiting for her husband's age-peers to die. The author references this as an application of masalih mursalah (considerations of public interest), a principle accepted by the Hanafi school. This shows ijtihad based on broader social welfare.

The third and most detailed example concerns the conquered lands of Iraq and Egypt. The conquering soldiers demanded distribution as war booty (ghanimah), citing Quranic verses about spoils. Umar disagreed, classifying the lands as fay' (public property) belonging to the entire Muslim community, present and future. He based this on a comprehensive reading of Quranic texts and the public interest of maintaining agricultural productivity and revenue for the treasury. He imposed kharaj tax on the original owners, keeping the lands productive.

The passage concludes by noting that Umar established the judiciary as an independent institution, and that his jurisprudential method became the model for the 'Ahl al-Ra'y' (School of Opinion) in Iraq, which later influenced the Hanafi school through Ibn Mas'ud and his students.

Key Terms
حد السرقة (hadd al-sariqah): The fixed punishment for theft: amputation of the hand, subject to conditions.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment left to the judge's discretion when hadd is not applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition of the hadd for theft did Umar consider to be absent during the Year of Ramadah?
A) The thief must be an adult.
B) The theft must be from a secure place.
C) The thief must not be compelled by necessity.
D) The stolen property must exceed a certain value.

Key Takeaway
The hadd for theft is conditional; necessity creates a shubha that blocks its application, as exemplified by Umar's ijtihad.

Umar's Ijtihad: The Missing Person's Wife After Four Years
Analysis of Umar's ijtihad on missing person's wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Umar's ruling on the wife of a missing person and identify how it illustrates the principle of maslahah mursalah.

Core Idea
Umar allowed remarriage after four years based on public interest, not on direct textual evidence.

When a husband goes missing, his wife faces prolonged uncertainty. Caliph Umar ruled that she may remarry after four years of waiting, without requiring proof of death. This ijtihad was not based on explicit Quran or hadith for this specific duration, but on consideration of the greater good (maslahah). This lesson examines that reasoning and its acceptance in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the problem
The wife of a missing person is in limbo: she cannot remarry as long as the marriage exists, but she cannot prove her husband's death. The default rule requires waiting until death is confirmed or until peers die (as per some earlier views).
The passage mentions 'موت لداته' (death of his age peers) as another method.
2
Identify Umar's ruling
Umar allowed the wife to remarry after four years of waiting, without needing verification of death. This was a departure from the earlier practice.
The four-year period is analogous to the waiting period for a woman whose husband is imprisoned or missing in war? Not specified here.
3
Understand the rationale (maslahah)
Umar's decision aimed to prevent hardship on the wife and society. Prolonged waiting can lead to psychological and social harm. This is an application of maslahah mursalah (unrestricted public interest).
Maslahah mursalah is a source of law in the Maliki school but accepted by Hanafis under certain conditions, especially when there is no contradictory text.
4
Connect to Hanafi usul
The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to achieve public interest in cases like this. Umar's precedent is cited as support for rule-making based on necessity and welfare.
This example is often used in discussions of 'consideration of public interest' in Hanafi legal theory.

Source Text

٣ - وكذلك إطلاقه النكاح لزوجة المفقود بعد أربع سنين من فقده دون انتظار تحقق وفاته أو موت لداته - أي أقرانه في العمر ـ كما سلف ذكره في بحث المصالح المرسلة. (ر: ف ١٢/٥).
٤ ـ وكذلك اجتهاد عمر في منع تقسيم أراضي سواد العراق وأراضي مصر على المجاهدين الفاتحين لها الذين طالبوا بتقسيمها بينهم كما تقسم الغنائم الحربية، بعد تخميسها محتجين بظواهر بعض نصوص القرآن والسنة في حقوق المجاهدين الغانمين في الغنيمة؛ فإنهم أرادوا اعتبار أراضي البلاد المفتوحة كالغنائم الحربية توزع على الجيش الفاتح.

فأبقى عمر الأرضين لأهليها، وطرح عليها ضريبة الخراج؛ لأن ذلك أصلح لإحيائها وأعم وأدوم لنفعها وريعها (1).
هذا، وإن عمر هو أول أنشأ منصب القضاء مستقلاً من عن غيره من الأعمال، وكان قبل ذلك تابعاً لولاية الأمراء والعمال، ومن جملة وظائفهم.
وإن طريقة عمر الفقهية كانت حجة خالدة لمدرسة «أهل الرأي» في العراق من فقهاء الطبقات التالية التي سيأتي ذكرها فقد تأثر هؤلاء بفقه ابن مسعود في الكوفة، وكان هو يسير على طريقة عمر رضي الله عنهما.
(۱) روي من حديث عائشة، ومن حديث علي ومن حديث أبي هريرة، أما حديث عائشة فأخرجه الترمذي (١٤٢٤) في الحدود، بلفظ: ادرؤوا الحدود عن المسلمين ما استطعتم فإن كان له مخرج فخلوا سبيله، فإن الإمام أن يخطىء في العفو خير من يخطىء في العقوبة وضعف رفعه وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك ٣٨٤/٤، وقال: صحيح الإسناد، وتعقبه الذهبي. وأما حديث علي فأخرجه الدارقطني ٨٤/٣ وأما حديث أبي هريرة فأخرجه ابن ماجه (٢٥٤٥) بلفظ : ادفعوا الحدود ما وجدتم له مدفعاً». وأخرجه ابن عدي في جزء له عن ابن عباس مرفوعاً بلفظ : ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات وأقيلوا الكرام عثراتهم إلا في حد من حدود الله» (ر:ف ٤/٤٩).
(۱) فصلنا اجتهاد عمر في شأن الأراضي المفتوحة في الملحق بهذا الفصل.
Chapter (11) Second Cycle: The Era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs
2 - Likewise, Umar's ijtihad in suspending the execution of the hadd punishment for theft on thieves in the year of famine called (Year of Ramadah); and his sufficing with tazir (discretionary punishment) for the thief instead of cutting off his hand. This from Umar is not a suspension of the legal hadd for theft, but is a wise ijtihad from him in applying the conditions of this punishment, because among its conditions legally is that the thief not be compelled to theft by necessity. So Umar considered in that a general doubt (shubha) that they were stealing due to compelling necessity, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts (shubuhat).' (1)
3 - Likewise, his permitting marriage for the wife of a missing person after four years of his disappearance without waiting for verification of his death or the death of his peers - i.e., his age mates - as previously mentioned in the discussion of considerations of public interest (masalih mursalah). (See: f 12/5).
4 - Likewise, Umar's ijtihad in preventing the division of the lands of Sawad of Iraq and the lands of Egypt among the conquering mujahideen who demanded their division among themselves as war booty is divided, after taking one-fifth, arguing with the apparent meanings of some Quranic and Sunnah texts regarding the rights of mujahideen to spoils; for they intended to consider the lands of the conquered territories as war booty distributed to the conquering army.
And Umar went contrary to their opinion, and he considered the lands as fay' (public property) to which the rights of Muslims in general, present and future, are attached, taking care of the interest of generations and their rights in the treasury, according to what sound consideration of the totality of Quranic texts indicates, not some of them without others.
So Umar left the lands to their owners, and imposed upon them the kharaj tax; because that was more beneficial for their cultivation and more comprehensive and lasting for their benefit and yield (1).
Moreover, Umar was the first to establish the position of judiciary independently from other offices, whereas before it was subordinate to the governors and officials, and part of their functions.
And indeed, Umar's jurisprudential method was an enduring proof for the school of 'Ahl al-Ra'y' in Iraq among the jurists of the following generations who will be mentioned later; for these were influenced by the fiqh of Ibn Mas'ud in Kufa, and he followed the method of Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both).
(1) Narrated from the hadith of Aisha, and from the hadith of Ali, and from the hadith of Abu Hurairah. As for the hadith of Aisha, it is recorded by al-Tirmidhi (1424) in the Book of Hudud with the wording: 'Avert the hudud from the Muslims as much as you can; if there is a way out, let him go, for the Imam to err in pardon is better than to err in punishment.' And he considered its raising (to the Prophet) weak. Al-Hakim recorded it in al-Mustadrak (4/384) and said: 'The chain is authentic,' but al-Dhahabi criticized it. As for the hadith of Ali, it is recorded by al-Daraqutni (3/84). As for the hadith of Abu Hurairah, it is recorded by Ibn Majah (2545) with the wording: 'Avert the hudud as long as you find a way to avert them.' And Ibn Adi recorded in a part of his work from Ibn Abbas with a raised chain: 'Avert the hudud by doubts and overlook the slips of the noble except in a hadd from the limits of Allah.' (See: f 4/49).
(1) We have detailed Umar's ijtihad regarding the conquered lands in the appendix to this chapter.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work presents a historical and legal analysis of three famous ijtihadat of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA). The author classifies this as part of the 'second cycle' of Islamic jurisprudence: the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The passage serves to illustrate how early juristic reasoning (ijtihad) operated within the framework of Sharia by applying general principles and conditions rather than merely following literal texts.

The first example concerns the suspension of the hadd punishment for theft during the severe famine known as 'Year of Ramadah.' Umar refrained from amputating thieves' hands and instead imposed a lesser discretionary punishment (ta'zir). The author clarifies that this was not an abandonment of the divine penalty but a proper application of its conditions: one condition is that the thief not be compelled by necessity. Umar considered the widespread famine as creating a general doubt (shubha) about criminal intent, relying on the Prophetic instruction to avert hudud by doubts. This demonstrates ijtihad based on textual evidence (the hadith) and causal reasoning ('illa).

The second example is Umar's ruling on the wife of a missing person. He allowed her to remarry after four years of waiting, without requiring proof of death or waiting for her husband's age-peers to die. The author references this as an application of masalih mursalah (considerations of public interest), a principle accepted by the Hanafi school. This shows ijtihad based on broader social welfare.

The third and most detailed example concerns the conquered lands of Iraq and Egypt. The conquering soldiers demanded distribution as war booty (ghanimah), citing Quranic verses about spoils. Umar disagreed, classifying the lands as fay' (public property) belonging to the entire Muslim community, present and future. He based this on a comprehensive reading of Quranic texts and the public interest of maintaining agricultural productivity and revenue for the treasury. He imposed kharaj tax on the original owners, keeping the lands productive.

The passage concludes by noting that Umar established the judiciary as an independent institution, and that his jurisprudential method became the model for the 'Ahl al-Ra'y' (School of Opinion) in Iraq, which later influenced the Hanafi school through Ibn Mas'ud and his students.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A missing person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose death is not confirmed.
مصالح مرسلة (masalih mursalah): Considerations of public interest not explicitly mentioned in the primary sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What source of law did Umar's ijtihad on the missing person's wife primarily rely on?
A) Quranic text
B) Sunnah of the Prophet
C) Ijma of the Companions
D) Maslahah mursalah (public interest)

Key Takeaway
When primary sources are silent, ijtihad based on public interest (maslahah) can establish rulings, as Umar did for the missing person's wife.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Umar's Ijtihad: Conquered Lands as Fay' Not Ghanimah
Analysis of Umar's ijtihad on conquered lands as fay'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Umar's reasoning for treating conquered lands as fay' rather than ghanimah and evaluate the Hanafi acceptance of this ijtihad.

Core Idea
Umar prioritized long-term community interest over immediate distribution, using a comprehensive reading of Quranic texts.

When the Muslim army conquered vast agricultural lands in Iraq and Egypt, the soldiers demanded distribution as war booty. Caliph Umar disagreed, keeping the lands under their original owners and imposing kharaj tax. This decision was based on a holistic understanding of Quranic principles regarding fay' and consideration of future generations. This lesson unpacks Umar's reasoning and its lasting impact on Islamic public finance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the demand of the soldiers
The conquerors cited the general Quranic rules of ghanimah (spoils) that allow distribution among fighters after taking one-fifth. They treated the lands as movable booty.
The Quran says 'And know that whatever you take as spoils, for Allah is one-fifth thereof...' (8:41).
2
Identify Umar's classification
Umar considered the lands as fay' (property acquired without war, or property belonging to the entire community) rather than ghanimah. This meant they were not to be divided but held in trust for all Muslims.
Fay' is mentioned in Quran 59:7: 'What Allah gave as spoil to His Messenger from the people of the towns...' implying it is for the community.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Umar used a comprehensive approach: he looked at the totality of Quranic verses, not just those on spoils. He considered the long-term public interest: dividing the lands would reduce agricultural productivity and deprive future generations of revenue. Keeping them productive under existing owners and taxing them (kharaj) would benefit the treasury indefinitely.
This is an example of ijtihad based on maqasid (objectives) and maslahah.
4
Connect to Hanafi usul
Umar's precedent is a cornerstone of Hanafi rulings on conquered lands. The school distinguishes between lands conquered by force (which become fay' in Umar's view) and movable spoils. The ruler has discretion for public welfare.
This ruling influenced later Islamic state finance and the Hanafi position on land tenure.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work presents a historical and legal analysis of three famous ijtihadat of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA). The author classifies this as part of the 'second cycle' of Islamic jurisprudence: the era of the Rightly Guided Caliphs. The second example is Umar's ruling on the wife of a missing person. Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Property acquired from non-Muslims without actual fighting, often considered public property.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on non-Muslim owners of conquered lands, or later on all agricultural land under Islamic state.
غنيمة (ghanimah): Movable spoils of war distributed among fighters after taking one-fifth.

What was the primary basis for Umar's classification of conquered lands as fay' rather than ghanimah?
A) A specific Quranic verse that explicitly says all lands are fay'.
B) A hadith from the Prophet.
C) A comprehensive reading of Quranic texts and consideration of public interest.

Key Takeaway
Umar's ijtihad on conquered lands demonstrates that public interest and holistic textual interpretation can override apparent meanings of specific verses.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Umar's Juristic Legacy and the Rise of Ahl al-Ra'y
Summary of Umar's overall juristic impact

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of Umar's ijtihad methodology and explain how it influenced the emergence of the Hanafi school of 'Ahl al-Ra'y.'

Core Idea
Umar's reasoned approach to ijtihad became the prototype for the Hanafi school's emphasis on opinion and analogy.

The passage concludes by noting that Umar was the first to establish an independent judiciary and that his jurisprudential method served as an enduring proof for the 'Ahl al-Ra'y' (School of Opinion) in Iraq. This school, which later evolved into the Hanafi madhhab, was characterized by its use of ra'y (reasoned opinion) and qiyas. This lesson traces Umar's influence on the development of Hanafi legal thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify Umar's institutional contribution
Umar was the first to separate the judiciary from the executive, creating an independent position of qadi. This professionalized legal decision-making.
Previously, governors also acted as judges.
2
Recognize Umar's methodological contribution
Umar's ijtihad was characterized by consideration of public interest, comprehensive textual analysis, and application of conditions (like shubha). This balanced textualism with rational reasoning.
The three examples in the passage illustrate this.
3
Connect to the Kufan school
The 'Ahl al-Ra'y' in Iraq, particularly in Kufa, studied under Ibn Mas'ud, who followed Umar's method. They emphasized ra'y and qiyas more than their Medinan counterparts.
Ibn Mas'ud was a student of Umar and a key figure in the Kufan school.
4
Link to the Hanafi school
Abu Hanifa and his students systematized this approach, developing usul that incorporated istihsan, 'urf, and qiyas, while still relying on primary texts. Umar's precedents are frequently cited in Hanafi works as authoritative.
The passage calls Umar's method 'حجة خالدة' (eternal proof) for Ahl al-Ra'y.

Source Text
